النظر الثالث : في المغلظات في القياس وفيه فصول
أسباب الغلط
وهي
الفصل الأول: في حصر مثارات الغلط اعلم أن المقدمات القياسية إذا ترتبت من حيث صورتها على ضرب منتج من الأشكال الثلاثة، وتفصلت منها الحدود الثلاثة أولاً، وهي الأجزاء الأولى، إذ تميزت المقدمتان الأجزاء الثواني، وكانت المقدمات صادقة وغير النتيجة وأعرف منها، كان اللازم منها بالضرورة حقاً لا ريب فيه، والذي لا يحصل منه الحق فإنما لا يحصل لخلل في هذه الجهات التي ذكرناها، إما لخروجه عن الأشكال أو لخروجه عن الضروب المنتجة منها، أو لعدم التمايز في الحدود أو في المقدمات أو لإدراج النتيجة في المقدمات فلا تكون غيرها، أو لأن النتيجة تكون متقدمة على إحدى المقدمات في المعرفة فلا تكون المقدمة أعرف من النتيجة فهذه سبع مثارات؛ فلنشرح كلّ واحدٍ بمثال حتى يَتَيَسَّر الاحتراز عنه فنقول :
المثار الأول : أن لا تكون على شكل من الأشكال الثلاثة، بأن لا يكون من الحدود حد مشترك إما موضوع فيهما أو محمول أو موضوع لأحدهما محمول للآخر، فإذا انتفى الاشتراك حقيقة ولفظاً لم يغلط الذهن فيه، فإن ذلك يظهر وإنما يغلط إذا وجد ما هو مشترك لفظاً مع اختلاف المعنى، ولذلك وجب تحقيق القول الألفاظ المشتركة لا سيّما ما يشتبه منها بالمتواطئة، ويعسر فيها درك الفرق، وهو مثار عظيم للأغاليط . وقد ذكرنا تفصيل ذلك على الإيجاز في كتاب «مقدمات القياس»، إلَّا أنا لم نذكر ثمّ إلَّا الألفاظ التي لا يتحد معناها، وقد يكون الاشتراك سببه النظم والترتيب للألفاظ لا نفس الألفاظ، ونحن نذكر من أمثلتها أربعة : الأول : ما ينشأ من مواضع الوقف والابتداء كما ذكرنا من قوله تعالى: ﴿ إِلَّا الله وَالرَّاسِخُونَ فِي الْعِلْمِ إذ له معنيان مختلفان فيطلق أمثاله في إحدى المقدمتين بمعنى، وفي الثاني بمعنى آخر، فيبطل الحد المشترك، ويظن أن ثمّ حد مشترك . الثاني : تردّدِ الضمائر بين أشياء متعددة تحتمل الانصراف إليها كقولك : كلّ ما علمه العاقل فهو كما علمه والعاقل يعلم الحجر فهو كالحجر، فإن قولك : فهو، متردّد بين أن يكون راجعاً إلى العاقل أو إلى المعقول، ويسلم في المقدمة على أنه راجع إلى المعقول، ويلبس في النتيجة فيخيل رجوعه إلى العاقل . الثالث : تردّد الحروف الناسقة بين معنيين تصدق في أحدهما وتكذب في الآخر كقوله : الخمسة زوج وفرد، وهو صادق فيظن أنه يصدق قولنا : إنه زوج وفرد معاً، وسببه اشتباه دلالة الواو فإنه يدلّ على جمع الأجزاء، إذ تقول : الإنسان عظم ولحم، أي فيه عظم ولحم، ويدلّ على جمع الأوصاف كقولنا : الإنسان حي وجسم، فإذن يصدق ما ذكرناه في الخمسة بطريق جمع الأجزاء لا بطريق جمع الصفات، واللفظ كاللفظ . الرابع : تردّد الصفة بین أن تكون صفة للموضوع وصفة للمحمول المذكور قبله فإنا قد نقول : زيد بصير أي ليس بضرير . وتقول : زيد طبيب . وإذا نظمنا فقلنا : زيد طبيب بصير ظنّ أنه بصير في الطب، وهذه الألفاظ تصدق مفرقة وتصدق مجموعة على أحد التأويلين دون الآخر، وأمثال ذلك مما يكثر ويرتفع شكل القياس من حيث لا يعرف وفيما ذكرناه غنية به
المثار الثاني : ألّا يكون على ضرب منتج من جملة ضروب الأشكال الثلاثة . مثاله قولك : قليل من الناس كاتب وكل كاتب عاقل فقليل من الناس عاقل. وهذه النتيجة صادقة إن لم ترد بإثبات القليل نفي الكثير فإن الكثير إذا كان عاقلاً ففيه القليل، وإن أريد به أن القليل فقط هو كاتب وعاقل اختلط نظم القياس، إذ كان
Third discussion: On fallacies in the syllogism. It contains sections. Causes of fallacy. They are: Chapter one: On enumerating the sources of fallacy. Know that when syllogistic premises are arranged in terms of their form according to a productive mode from the three figures, and the three terms are first distinguished from them — which are the first parts — since the two premises are distinguished as the second parts, and the premises are true and other than the conclusion and more known than it, then what necessarily follows from them is true without doubt. That from which the truth is not obtained is only not obtained due to a defect in these aspects that we have mentioned: either due to its exiting from the figures, or due to its exiting from the productive moods among them, or due to lack of differentiation in the terms or in the premises, or due to the inclusion of the conclusion in the premises so it is not other than them, or because the conclusion is prior to one of the premises in knowledge so the premise is not more known than the conclusion. These are seven sources. We shall explain each one with an example so that avoidance of it becomes easy, and we say: The first source: that it is not in one of the three figures, namely that among the terms there is no common term either subject in both, or predicate, or subject of one and predicate of the other. If the commonality in reality and in wording is absent, the mind is not deceived by it, for that is apparent. Rather, it is deceived when there exists what is common in wording with difference of meaning. Therefore, investigation of ambiguous words is necessary, especially those that are similar to univocal ones, and discerning the difference is difficult, and it is a great source of fallacies. We have mentioned the detail of that summarily in the book 'Muqaddimat al-Qiyas', except that we did not mention there except words whose meaning is not unified. And commonality may be caused by the arrangement and order of words, not the words themselves. We mention four examples of them: The first: what arises from positions of pause and beginning, as we mentioned from His saying, exalted: 'Except Allah and those firmly rooted in knowledge' – it has two different meanings. So its likes are used in one premise with one meaning, and in the second with another meaning, thus the common term is invalidated, and it is thought that there is a common term. The second: ambiguity of pronouns among multiple things that can be referred to, as in your saying: 'Everything the rational being knows is as he knows it, and the rational being knows the stone, so it is like the stone.' For your saying 'so it' is ambiguous between referring to the rational being or to the known object. It is accepted in the premise as referring to the known object, and it is confused in the conclusion, making it appear to refer to the rational being. The third: ambiguity of coordinating particles between two meanings, true in one and false in the other, as in his saying: 'Five is even and odd' – it is true, so it is thought that our statement 'it is even and odd together' is true. The cause is confusion in the indication of the waw, for it indicates gathering of parts, as when we say 'Man is bone and flesh', meaning he has bone and flesh, and it indicates gathering of attributes, as our saying 'Man is living and a body'. So our statement about five is true in the way of gathering parts, not in the way of gathering attributes, and the wording is the same. The fourth: ambiguity of the adjective between being an adjective of the subject and an adjective of the predicate mentioned before it, for we may say 'Zayd is sighted', i.e. not blind, and you say 'Zayd is a doctor'. When we arrange and say 'Zayd is a sighted doctor', it is thought that he is sighted in medicine. These words are true separately and true together according to one of the two interpretations but not the other. Examples like that are many, and the form of the syllogism is lifted without being known. In what we have mentioned there is sufficiency. The second source: that it is not in a productive mood among the total of the moods of the three figures. Its example is your saying: 'Some people are writers, and every writer is rational, so some people are rational.' This conclusion is true if by affirming 'some' you do not intend to deny 'many', for if many are rational then among them is some. But if by it is intended that only some are writers and rational, then the arrangement of the syllogism is confused, for it would be ...
Source pages p. 103

Commentary
This passage is an introduction to the study of fallacies in syllogistic reasoning. The author begins by stating the conditions for a valid syllogism: the premises must be arranged in a productive mood of one of the three figures, the three terms must be distinguished, the premises must be true, distinct from the conclusion, and more known than the conclusion. When these conditions are met, the conclusion is necessarily true. Conversely, any failure to obtain truth arises from a defect in one of these aspects. The author enumerates seven such defects or 'sources' (matharat): (1) the syllogism is not in one of the three valid figures, (2) it is not in a productive mood, (3) the terms are not properly distinguished, (4) the premises are not distinguished, (5) the conclusion is included in the premises (petitio principii), (6) the conclusion is more known than one of the premises, and (7) the premises are not more known than the conclusion. The author then promises to explain each with examples.

The first source is elaborated in detail: the absence of a common term between the two premises. If there is no shared term in reality or wording, the fallacy is obvious. The trouble arises when a term is shared in wording but differs in meaning. Hence, ambiguous words (al-alfaz al-mushtaraka) must be carefully analyzed, especially those that resemble univocal terms. The author notes that ambiguity can also arise from the arrangement of words, not just individual words. Four sub-cases are given:
1. Ambiguity arising from positions of pause and beginning in speech, illustrated by the Quranic verse 'إلا الله والراسخون في العلم' (Except Allah and those firmly rooted in knowledge). Depending on how one places the pause, the meaning changes, leading to a deceptive common term.
2. Ambiguity of pronouns, where a pronoun could refer to different antecedents. Example: 'Everything the rational being knows is as he knows it; the rational being knows the stone; so it is like the stone.' The pronoun 'it' (هو) could refer to the rational being or the known object, causing confusion.
3. Ambiguity of coordinating particles, specifically the letter waw which can indicate either a conjunction of parts (as in 'man is bone and flesh', meaning he has both) or a conjunction of attributes (as in 'man is living and a body', meaning he possesses both attributes). The example 'five is even and odd' is true only in the sense of having both even and odd parts, not both attributes, but the wording suggests the latter.
4. Ambiguity of adjectives that could qualify either the subject or the predicate. Example: 'Zayd is a sighted doctor' – 'sighted' might be taken as a property of Zayd in general or specifically as a doctor. The sentence is true separately but ambiguous when combined.

The second source is then introduced: not being a productive mood. The example given is: 'Some people are writers; every writer is rational; so some people are rational.' This syllogism is in the third figure (Darapti) and is actually productive if 'some people' in the conclusion refers to the same individuals as in the premise. However, the fallacy arises if one intends to deny that many people are rational, because the conclusion 'some people are rational' is compatible with many being rational. If one mistakenly interprets the conclusion as asserting that only some people are rational (excluding others), then the syllogism becomes invalid. The author's explanation is cut off at this point, but the key point is that the mood must be correctly identified and not confused by existential import or other misinterpretations.

According to the passage, how many sources of fallacy are enumerated?
A) 5

Key Takeaway
Seven sources of fallacy arise from defects in figure, mood, terms, premises, conclusion inclusion, epistemic priority, and premise knowledge.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Identify which of the seven sources would be violated if a syllogism uses a premise that is less known than the conclusion. Provide an example from fiqh.

Sophistry and Fallacies
intermediate
10 min
First Source: Absence of a Genuine Common Term
Author explains the first source of fallacy and its four subtypes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify fallacies caused by ambiguous common terms, including the four subtypes: pause/beginning, pronouns, particles, and adjectives.

Core Idea
Ambiguous wording can create the illusion of a shared term when none exists, leading to fallacy.

The first source of fallacy occurs when the two premises lack a genuine common term, but a word appears shared due to ambiguity. The author details four types of such ambiguity, providing examples including a Quranic verse. Mastering these helps avoid deceptive arguments.

Guided Path
1
Understand the first source
The first source is that the syllogism is not in a valid figure because there is no real common term. The common term may appear shared due to ambiguous wording.
2
Learn the four subtypes
The author lists: 1) ambiguity from pause/beginning, 2) pronoun ambiguity, 3) coordinating particle ambiguity, 4) adjective ambiguity.
Each is illustrated with an example.
3
Analyze examples
For each subtype, note how the same wording can be interpreted differently in the two premises, breaking the common term.
Focus on the Quranic example for pause/beginning and the 'five is even and odd' for particles.

Commentary
This passage is an introduction to the study of fallacies in syllogistic reasoning. The author begins by stating the conditions for a valid syllogism: the premises must be arranged in a productive mood of one of the three figures, the three terms must be distinguished, the premises must be true, distinct from the conclusion, and more known than the conclusion. When these conditions are met, the conclusion is necessarily true. Conversely, any failure to obtain truth arises from a defect in one of these aspects. The author enumerates seven such defects or 'sources' (matharat): (1) the syllogism is not in one of the three valid figures, (2) it is not in a productive mood, (3) the terms are not properly distinguished, (4) the premises are not distinguished, (5) the conclusion is included in the premises (petitio principii), (6) the conclusion is more known than one of the premises, and (7) the premises are not more known than the conclusion. The author then promises to explain each with examples.

The first source is elaborated in detail: the absence of a common term between the two premises. If there is no shared term in reality or wording, the fallacy is obvious. The trouble arises when a term is shared in wording but differs in meaning. Hence, ambiguous words (al-alfaz al-mushtaraka) must be carefully analyzed, especially those that resemble univocal terms. The author notes that ambiguity can also arise from the arrangement of words, not just individual words. Four sub-cases are given:
1. Ambiguity arising from positions of pause and beginning in speech, illustrated by the Quranic verse 'إلا الله والراسخون في العلم' (Except Allah and those firmly rooted in knowledge). Depending on how one places the pause, the meaning changes, leading to a deceptive common term.
2. Ambiguity of pronouns, where a pronoun could refer to different antecedents. Example: 'Everything the rational being knows is as he knows it; the rational being knows the stone; so it is like the stone.' The pronoun 'it' (هو) could refer to the rational being or the known object, causing confusion.
3. Ambiguity of coordinating particles, specifically the letter waw which can indicate either a conjunction of parts (as in 'man is bone and flesh', meaning he has both) or a conjunction of attributes (as in 'man is living and a body', meaning he possesses both attributes). The example 'five is even and odd' is true only in the sense of having both even and odd parts, not both attributes, but the wording suggests the latter.
4. Ambiguity of adjectives that could qualify either the subject or the predicate. Example: 'Zayd is a sighted doctor' – 'sighted' might be taken as a property of Zayd in general or specifically as a doctor. The sentence is true separately but ambiguous when combined.

The second source is then introduced: not being a productive mood. The example given is: 'Some people are writers; every writer is rational; so some people are rational.' This syllogism is in the third figure (Darapti) and is actually productive if 'some people' in the conclusion refers to the same individuals as in the premise. However, the fallacy arises if one intends to deny that many people are rational, because the conclusion 'some people are rational' is compatible with many being rational. If one mistakenly interprets the conclusion as asserting that only some people are rational (excluding others), then the syllogism becomes invalid. The author's explanation is cut off at this point, but the key point is that the mood must be correctly identified and not confused by existential import or other misinterpretations.

Key Terms
المشتركة
المتواطئة
تردد الضمائر
تردد الحروف الناسقة

Key Takeaway
The first source of fallacy is the absence of a genuine common term, often hidden by ambiguous wording; four subtypes are pause/beginning, pronouns, particles, and adjectives.

Second Source: Not in a Productive Mood
Author explains the second source of fallacy and gives an example.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to determine whether a syllogism is in a productive mood and identify when existential import or misinterpretation causes it to be fallacious.

Core Idea
Even in a valid figure, a syllogism can be unproductive if the mood is invalid or the conclusion is misinterpreted.

The second source of fallacy occurs when the syllogism is in a valid figure but not in a productive mood. The author gives the example 'Some people are writers; every writer is rational; so some people are rational.' This appears valid but can be fallacious if the existential import is misconstrued. Let's see why.

Guided Path
1
Identify the premises and conclusion
Minor premise: 'Some people are writers.' Major premise: 'Every writer is rational.' Conclusion: 'Some people are rational.'
2
Check figure and mood
This is a third-figure syllogism (Darapti): the middle term 'writer' is subject in major and predicate in minor. Both premises are affirmative, so it is productive.
Darapti requires both premises affirmative.
3
Understand the potential fallacy
The author notes the fallacy arises if one intends to deny that many people are rational. The conclusion 'some people are rational' is true even if many are rational. The fallacy is in thinking the conclusion implies 'only some', not 'at least some'.
This is a misinterpretation of existential import.

النظر الثالث : في المغلظات في القياس وفيه فصول

الفصل الأول: في حصر مثارات الغلط اعلم أن المقدمات القياسية إذا ترتبت من حيث صورتها على ضرب منتج من الأشكال الثلاثة، وتفصلت منها الحدود الثلاثة أولاً، وهي الأجزاء الأولى، إذ تميزت المقدمتان الأجزاء الثواني، وكانت المقدمات صادقة وغير النتيجة وأعرف منها، كان اللازم منها بالضرورة حقاً لا ريب فيه، والذي لا يحصل منه الحق فإنما لا يحصل لخلل في هذه الجهات التي ذكرناها، إما لخروجه عن الأشكال أو لخروجه عن الضروب المنتجة منها، أو لعدم التمايز في الحدود أو في المقدمات أو لإدراج النتيجة في المقدمات فلا تكون غيرها، أو لأن النتيجة تكون متقدمة على إحدى المقدمات في المعرفة فلا تكون المقدمة أعرف من النتيجة فهذه سبع مثارات؛ فلنشرح كلّ واحدٍ بمثال حتى يَتَيَسَّر الاحتراز عنه فنقول :
المثار الأول : أن لا تكون على شكل من الأشكال الثلاثة، بأن لا يكون من الحدود حد مشترك إما موضوع فيهما أو محمول أو موضوع لأحدهما محمول للآخر، فإذا انتفى الاشتراك حقيقة ولفظاً لم يغلط الذهن فيه، فإن ذلك يظهر وإنما يغلط إذا وجد ما هو مشترك لفظاً مع اختلاف المعنى، ولذلك وجب تحقيق القول الألفاظ المشتركة لا سيّما ما يشتبه منها بالمتواطئة، ويعسر فيها درك الفرق، وهو مثار عظيم للأغاليط . وقد ذكرنا تفصيل ذلك على الإيجاز في كتاب «مقدمات القياس»، إلَّا أنا لم نذكر ثمّ إلَّا الألفاظ التي لا يتحد معناها، وقد يكون الاشتراك سببه النظم والترتيب للألفاظ لا نفس الألفاظ، ونحن نذكر من أمثلتها أربعة : الأول : ما ينشأ من مواضع الوقف والابتداء كما ذكرنا من قوله تعالى: ﴿ إِلَّا الله وَالرَّاسِخُونَ فِي الْعِلْمِ إذ له معنيان مختلفان فيطلق أمثاله في إحدى المقدمتين بمعنى، وفي الثاني بمعنى آخر، فيبطل الحد المشترك، ويظن أن ثمّ حد مشترك . الثاني : تردّدِ الضمائر بين أشياء متعددة تحتمل الانصراف إليها كقولك : كلّ ما علمه العاقل فهو كما علمه والعاقل يعلم الحجر فهو كالحجر، فإن قولك : فهو، متردّد بين أن يكون راجعاً إلى العاقل أو إلى المعقول، ويسلم في المقدمة على أنه راجع إلى المعقول، ويلبس في النتيجة فيخيل رجوعه إلى العاقل . الثالث : تردّد الحروف الناسقة بين معنيين تصدق في أحدهما وتكذب في الآخر كقوله : الخمسة زوج وفرد، وهو صادق فيظن أنه يصدق قولنا : إنه زوج وفرد معاً، وسببه اشتباه دلالة الواو فإنه يدلّ على جمع الأجزاء، إذ تقول : الإنسان عظم ولحم، أي فيه عظم ولحم، ويدلّ على جمع الأوصاف كقولنا : الإنسان حي وجسم، فإذن يصدق ما ذكرناه في الخمسة بطريق جمع الأجزاء لا بطريق جمع الصفات، واللفظ كاللفظ . الرابع : تردّد الصفة بین أن تكون صفة للموضوع وصفة للمحمول المذكور قبله فإنا قد نقول : زيد بصير أي ليس بضرير . وتقول : زيد طبيب . وإذا نظمنا فقلنا : زيد طبيب بصير ظنّ أنه بصير في الطب، وهذه الألفاظ تصدق مفرقة وتصدق مجموعة على أحد التأويلين دون الآخر، وأمثال ذلك مما يكثر ويرتفع شكل القياس من حيث لا يعرف وفيما ذكرناه غنية به
المثار الثاني : ألّا يكون على ضرب منتج من جملة ضروب الأشكال الثلاثة . مثاله قولك : قليل من الناس كاتب وكل كاتب عاقل فقليل من الناس عاقل. وهذه النتيجة صادقة إن لم ترد بإثبات القليل نفي الكثير فإن الكثير إذا كان عاقلاً ففيه القليل، وإن أريد به أن القليل فقط هو كاتب وعاقل اختلط نظم القياس، إذ كان
Third discussion: On fallacies in the syllogism. It contains sections. Causes of fallacy. They are: Chapter one: On enumerating the sources of fallacy. Know that when syllogistic premises are arranged in terms of their form according to a productive mode from the three figures, and the three terms are first distinguished from them — which are the first parts — since the two premises are distinguished as the second parts, and the premises are true and other than the conclusion and more known than it, then what necessarily follows from them is true without doubt. That from which the truth is not obtained is only not obtained due to a defect in these aspects that we have mentioned: either due to its exiting from the figures, or due to its exiting from the productive moods among them, or due to lack of differentiation in the terms or in the premises, or due to the inclusion of the conclusion in the premises so it is not other than them, or because the conclusion is prior to one of the premises in knowledge so the premise is not more known than the conclusion. These are seven sources. We shall explain each one with an example so that avoidance of it becomes easy, and we say: The first source: that it is not in one of the three figures, namely that among the terms there is no common term either subject in both, or predicate, or subject of one and predicate of the other. If the commonality in reality and in wording is absent, the mind is not deceived by it, for that is apparent. Rather, it is deceived when there exists what is common in wording with difference of meaning. Therefore, investigation of ambiguous words is necessary, especially those that are similar to univocal ones, and discerning the difference is difficult, and it is a great source of fallacies. We have mentioned the detail of that summarily in the book 'Muqaddimat al-Qiyas', except that we did not mention there except words whose meaning is not unified. And commonality may be caused by the arrangement and order of words, not the words themselves. We mention four examples of them: The first: what arises from positions of pause and beginning, as we mentioned from His saying, exalted: 'Except Allah and those firmly rooted in knowledge' – it has two different meanings. So its likes are used in one premise with one meaning, and in the second with another meaning, thus the common term is invalidated, and it is thought that there is a common term. The second: ambiguity of pronouns among multiple things that can be referred to, as in your saying: 'Everything the rational being knows is as he knows it, and the rational being knows the stone, so it is like the stone.' For your saying 'so it' is ambiguous between referring to the rational being or to the known object. It is accepted in the premise as referring to the known object, and it is confused in the conclusion, making it appear to refer to the rational being. The third: ambiguity of coordinating particles between two meanings, true in one and false in the other, as in his saying: 'Five is even and odd' – it is true, so it is thought that our statement 'it is even and odd together' is true. The cause is confusion in the indication of the waw, for it indicates gathering of parts, as when we say 'Man is bone and flesh', meaning he has bone and flesh, and it indicates gathering of attributes, as our saying 'Man is living and a body'. So our statement about five is true in the way of gathering parts, not in the way of gathering attributes, and the wording is the same. The fourth: ambiguity of the adjective between being an adjective of the subject and an adjective of the predicate mentioned before it, for we may say 'Zayd is sighted', i.e. not blind, and you say 'Zayd is a doctor'. When we arrange and say 'Zayd is a sighted doctor', it is thought that he is sighted in medicine. These words are true separately and true together according to one of the two interpretations but not the other. Examples like that are many, and the form of the syllogism is lifted without being known. In what we have mentioned there is sufficiency. The second source: that it is not in a productive mood among the total of the moods of the three figures. Its example is your saying: 'Some people are writers, and every writer is rational, so some people are rational.' This conclusion is true if by affirming 'some' you do not intend to deny 'many', for if many are rational then among them is some. But if by it is intended that only some are writers and rational, then the arrangement of the syllogism is confused, for it would be ...
Source pages p. 103

Commentary
This passage is an introduction to the study of fallacies in syllogistic reasoning. The author begins by stating the conditions for a valid syllogism: the premises must be arranged in a productive mood of one of the three figures, the three terms must be distinguished, the premises must be true, distinct from the conclusion, and more known than the conclusion. When these conditions are met, the conclusion is necessarily true. Conversely, any failure to obtain truth arises from a defect in one of these aspects. The author enumerates seven such defects or 'sources' (matharat): (1) the syllogism is not in one of the three valid figures, (2) it is not in a productive mood, (3) the terms are not properly distinguished, (4) the premises are not distinguished, (5) the conclusion is included in the premises (petitio principii), (6) the conclusion is more known than one of the premises, and (7) the premises are not more known than the conclusion. The author then promises to explain each with examples.

The first source is elaborated in detail: the absence of a common term between the two premises. If there is no shared term in reality or wording, the fallacy is obvious. The trouble arises when a term is shared in wording but differs in meaning. Hence, ambiguous words (al-alfaz al-mushtaraka) must be carefully analyzed, especially those that resemble univocal terms. The author notes that ambiguity can also arise from the arrangement of words, not just individual words. Four sub-cases are given:
1. Ambiguity arising from positions of pause and beginning in speech, illustrated by the Quranic verse 'إلا الله والراسخون في العلم' (Except Allah and those firmly rooted in knowledge). Depending on how one places the pause, the meaning changes, leading to a deceptive common term.
2. Ambiguity of pronouns, where a pronoun could refer to different antecedents. Example: 'Everything the rational being knows is as he knows it; the rational being knows the stone; so it is like the stone.' The pronoun 'it' (هو) could refer to the rational being or the known object, causing confusion.
3. Ambiguity of coordinating particles, specifically the letter waw which can indicate either a conjunction of parts (as in 'man is bone and flesh', meaning he has both) or a conjunction of attributes (as in 'man is living and a body', meaning he possesses both attributes). The example 'five is even and odd' is true only in the sense of having both even and odd parts, not both attributes, but the wording suggests the latter.
4. Ambiguity of adjectives that could qualify either the subject or the predicate. Example: 'Zayd is a sighted doctor' – 'sighted' might be taken as a property of Zayd in general or specifically as a doctor. The sentence is true separately but ambiguous when combined.

The second source is then introduced: not being a productive mood. The example given is: 'Some people are writers; every writer is rational; so some people are rational.' This syllogism is in the third figure (Darapti) and is actually productive if 'some people' in the conclusion refers to the same individuals as in the premise. However, the fallacy arises if one intends to deny that many people are rational, because the conclusion 'some people are rational' is compatible with many being rational. If one mistakenly interprets the conclusion as asserting that only some people are rational (excluding others), then the syllogism becomes invalid. The author's explanation is cut off at this point, but the key point is that the mood must be correctly identified and not confused by existential import or other misinterpretations.

Key Terms
الضروب المنتجة
الأشكال الثلاثة

Key Terms from Source Passage

According to the text, what is the example given for the second source?
A) Some people are writers; every writer is rational; so some people are rational.
B) Every A is B; some C is A; so some C is B.
C) No A is B; some C is A; so some C is not B.
D) All men are mortal; Socrates is a man; so Socrates is mortal.

Key Takeaway
The second source is not being in a productive mood; even within a valid figure, the mood must be one of the productive ones for the conclusion to follow necessarily.

Given the syllogism 'No man is immortal; some rational beings are men; therefore some rational beings are not immortal', determine its figure, mood, and whether it is productive.

Fallacy of Ambiguous Homonyms
First type: ambiguity from homonyms (mushtarak lafzi).

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify when a homonym used with two distinct meanings in the premises invalidates a syllogism.

Core Idea
One word, two meanings: no common middle term.

This passage introduces the first type of linguistic ambiguity that destroys syllogistic validity. When a term has two different meanings, and it is used with one meaning in the first premise and another in the second, the middle term is not truly shared, and the conclusion does not follow. The author cites the Quranic verse 'Allah and those firmly rooted in knowledge' as an example, where the word 'rasikhun' (firmly rooted) can mean different things.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ambiguous word
Find the word that has more than one meaning. In the example, it is 'rasikhun' (firmly rooted) or the phrase 'Allah and those firmly rooted.'
The ambiguity is in the term itself, not its reference.
2
List the two distinct meanings
Determine the two meanings that the word can have. For 'rasikhun', it can mean 'those deeply learned in knowledge' or 'those firm in knowledge in a specific sense.'
The two meanings are different enough that the term is not univocal.
3
Check each premise's usage
In the first premise, the word is used with one meaning; in the second premise, with the other. The middle term appears the same but is actually different.
The common term (al-hadd al-mushtarak) is nullified.
4
Recognize the fallacy
Because the middle term is not univocal, the syllogism is invalid. One might think there is a common term, but it is an illusion.
This is a type of fallacy of equivocation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الله وَالرَّاسِخُونَ فِي الْعِلْمِ (1) إذ له معنيان مختلفان فيطلق أمثاله في إحدى المقدمتين بمعنى، وفي الثاني بمعنى آخر، فيبطل الحد المشترك، ويظن أن ثمّ حد مشترك . الثاني : تردّدِ الضمائر بين أشياء متعددة تحتمل الانصراف إليها كقولك : كلّ ما علمه العاقل فهو كما علمه والعاقل يعلم الحجر فهو كالحجر، فإن قولك : فهو، متردّد بين أن يكون راجعاً إلى العاقل أو إلى المعقول، ويسلم في المقدمة على أنه راجع إلى المعقول، ويلبس في النتيجة فيخيل رجوعه إلى العاقل . الثالث : تردّد الحروف الناسقة (2) بين معنيين تصدق في أحدهما وتكذب في الآخر كقوله : الخمسة زوج وفرد، وهو صادق فيظن أنه يصدق قولنا : إنه زوج وفرد معاً، وسببه اشتباه دلالة الواو فإنه يدلّ على جمع الأجزاء، إذ تقول : الإنسان عظم ولحم، أي فيه عظم ولحم، ويدلّ على جمع الأوصاف كقولنا : الإنسان حي وجسم، فإذن يصدق ما ذكرناه في الخمسة بطريق جمع الأجزاء لا بطريق جمع الصفات، واللفظ كاللفظ . الرابع : تردّد الصفة بین أن تكون صفة للموضوع وصفة للمحمول المذكور قبله فإنا قد نقول : زيد بصير أي ليس بضرير . وتقول : زيد طبيب . وإذا نظمنا فقلنا : زيد طبيب بصير ظنّ أنه بصير في الطب، وهذه الألفاظ تصدق مفرقة وتصدق مجموعة على أحد التأويلين دون الآخر، وأمثال ذلك مما يكثر ويرتفع شكل القياس من حيث لا يعرف وفيما ذكرناه غنية (3)
‘Allah and those firmly rooted in knowledge’—since it has two different meanings, so its likes are used in one premise with one meaning and in the second with another meaning, so the common term is nullified, and it is thought that there is a common term. Second: the ambiguity of pronouns among multiple things that admit reference to them, like your saying: ‘Everything that the rational knows, it is as he knows it, and the rational knows the stone, so it is like the stone.’ For your saying ‘it’ is ambiguous between referring to the rational or the known; it is accepted in the premise as referring to the known, but in the conclusion it is confused, making it seem to refer to the rational. Third: ambiguity of conjunctive letters (nasq) between two meanings true in one and false in the other, like his saying: ‘Five is even and odd,’ which is true, so one thinks that our saying ‘it is even and odd together’ is true. The cause is confusion in the meaning of ‘waw’, for it indicates collection of parts—as you say: ‘Man is bone and flesh,’ i.e. in him is bone and flesh—and it indicates collection of attributes—as our saying: ‘Man is living and body.’ So what we mentioned about five is true by way of collection of parts, not by way of collection of attributes, and the wording is the same. Fourth: ambiguity of the adjective between being an adjective of the subject or an adjective of the previously mentioned predicate; for we may say: ‘Zayd is seeing,’ i.e. not blind, and you say: ‘Zayd is a physician.’ If we compose and say: ‘Zayd is a physician, seeing,’ one thinks he is seeing in medicine. These words are true separately and true together on one of the two interpretations but not the other. And similar examples are many, and the form of the syllogism collapses without being known, and in what we have mentioned there is sufficiency.
Source pages p. 104

Commentary
This passage defines three types of linguistic ambiguity that invalidate syllogisms, though the author actually lists four: (1) ambiguous homonyms like the Quranic verse, (2) ambiguous pronouns, (3) ambiguous conjunctive particles, and (4) ambiguous adjectives. Each type shows how a term used in two different ways in premises leads to a false conclusion. The author illustrates each with examples. For pronouns, the word 'huwa' may refer to the rational agent or the known object, causing a shift in meaning. For particles, the conjunction 'waw' can mean composition of parts or composition of attributes, leading the hearer to think five is both even and odd simultaneously. For adjectives, describing Zayd as 'seeing' and 'physician' may mislead one into thinking he is skilled in medicine. The key lesson: preserving the same meaning across premises is essential for a valid syllogism.

Key Terms
الحد المشترك (al-hadd al-mushtarak): The common term (middle) that should be univocal but is ambiguous due to multiple meanings. [Note: In standard logic, hadd mushtarak refers to a homonymous term; here it specifically means the middle term losing its univocity.]

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What happens to the common term (al-hadd al-mushtarak) when a homonym is used with two meanings?
A) It becomes stronger.
B) It is nullified.
C) It becomes univocal.
D) It is preserved.

Key Takeaway
A homonym used with two meanings in the premises destroys the middle term and invalidates the syllogism.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Find an Arabic word with two distinct meanings (e.g., 'ayn meaning eye or spring). Construct a syllogism where the middle term is that word, used differently in each premise. Explain why the conclusion does not follow.

Fallacy of Ambiguous Pronouns
Second type: ambiguity of pronouns.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify when an ambiguous pronoun causes a fallacy in a syllogism.

Core Idea
A pronoun with two possible antecedents can create a false conclusion.

The second type of linguistic ambiguity involves pronouns. The author gives an example: 'Everything that the rational knows, it is as he knows it, and the rational knows the stone, so it is like the stone.' The pronoun 'huwa' (it/he) can refer to the rational agent or the known object. In the premise, it is taken to refer to the known object, but in the conclusion, it seems to refer to the rational agent, causing a fallacy.

Guided Path
1
Locate the ambiguous pronoun
Find the pronoun that has more than one possible antecedent. In the example, 'huwa' appears twice.
The pronoun is the same but its reference changes.
2
Identify the possible antecedents
List the nouns that the pronoun could refer to. Here, it could be 'al-aaqil' (the rational) or 'al-ma'qool' (the known).
The antecedents are different.
3
Check the premise usage
In the first premise, the pronoun is interpreted as referring to the known object. That interpretation makes the premise true.
The premise is accepted under one interpretation.
4
Examine the conclusion
In the conclusion, the pronoun is mistakenly taken to refer to the subject (the rational), making the conclusion false or invalid.
The shift in reference creates a false inference.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الله وَالرَّاسِخُونَ فِي الْعِلْمِ (1) إذ له معنيان مختلفان فيطلق أمثاله في إحدى المقدمتين بمعنى، وفي الثاني بمعنى آخر، فيبطل الحد المشترك، ويظن أن ثمّ حد مشترك . الثاني : تردّدِ الضمائر بين أشياء متعددة تحتمل الانصراف إليها كقولك : كلّ ما علمه العاقل فهو كما علمه والعاقل يعلم الحجر فهو كالحجر، فإن قولك : فهو، متردّد بين أن يكون راجعاً إلى العاقل أو إلى المعقول، ويسلم في المقدمة على أنه راجع إلى المعقول، ويلبس في النتيجة فيخيل رجوعه إلى العاقل . الثالث : تردّد الحروف الناسقة (2) بين معنيين تصدق في أحدهما وتكذب في الآخر كقوله : الخمسة زوج وفرد، وهو صادق فيظن أنه يصدق قولنا : إنه زوج وفرد معاً، وسببه اشتباه دلالة الواو فإنه يدلّ على جمع الأجزاء، إذ تقول : الإنسان عظم ولحم، أي فيه عظم ولحم، ويدلّ على جمع الأوصاف كقولنا : الإنسان حي وجسم، فإذن يصدق ما ذكرناه في الخمسة بطريق جمع الأجزاء لا بطريق جمع الصفات، واللفظ كاللفظ . الرابع : تردّد الصفة بین أن تكون صفة للموضوع وصفة للمحمول المذكور قبله فإنا قد نقول : زيد بصير أي ليس بضرير . وتقول : زيد طبيب . وإذا نظمنا فقلنا : زيد طبيب بصير ظنّ أنه بصير في الطب، وهذه الألفاظ تصدق مفرقة وتصدق مجموعة على أحد التأويلين دون الآخر، وأمثال ذلك مما يكثر ويرتفع شكل القياس من حيث لا يعرف وفيما ذكرناه غنية (3)
‘Allah and those firmly rooted in knowledge’—since it has two different meanings, so its likes are used in one premise with one meaning and in the second with another meaning, so the common term is nullified, and it is thought that there is a common term. Second: the ambiguity of pronouns among multiple things that admit reference to them, like your saying: ‘Everything that the rational knows, it is as he knows it, and the rational knows the stone, so it is like the stone.’ For your saying ‘it’ is ambiguous between referring to the rational or the known; it is accepted in the premise as referring to the known, but in the conclusion it is confused, making it seem to refer to the rational. Third: ambiguity of conjunctive letters (nasq) between two meanings true in one and false in the other, like his saying: ‘Five is even and odd,’ which is true, so one thinks that our saying ‘it is even and odd together’ is true. The cause is confusion in the meaning of ‘waw’, for it indicates collection of parts—as you say: ‘Man is bone and flesh,’ i.e. in him is bone and flesh—and it indicates collection of attributes—as our saying: ‘Man is living and body.’ So what we mentioned about five is true by way of collection of parts, not by way of collection of attributes, and the wording is the same. Fourth: ambiguity of the adjective between being an adjective of the subject or an adjective of the previously mentioned predicate; for we may say: ‘Zayd is seeing,’ i.e. not blind, and you say: ‘Zayd is a physician.’ If we compose and say: ‘Zayd is a physician, seeing,’ one thinks he is seeing in medicine. These words are true separately and true together on one of the two interpretations but not the other. And similar examples are many, and the form of the syllogism collapses without being known, and in what we have mentioned there is sufficiency.
Source pages p. 104

Commentary
This passage defines three types of linguistic ambiguity that invalidate syllogisms, though the author actually lists four: (1) ambiguous homonyms like the Quranic verse, (2) ambiguous pronouns, (3) ambiguous conjunctive particles, and (4) ambiguous adjectives. Each type shows how a term used in two different ways in premises leads to a false conclusion. The author illustrates each with examples. For pronouns, the word 'huwa' may refer to the rational agent or the known object, causing a shift in meaning. For particles, the conjunction 'waw' can mean composition of parts or composition of attributes, leading the hearer to think five is both even and odd simultaneously. For adjectives, describing Zayd as 'seeing' and 'physician' may mislead one into thinking he is skilled in medicine. The key lesson: preserving the same meaning across premises is essential for a valid syllogism.

Key Terms
تردد الضمائر (taraddud al-dama'ir): Ambiguity of pronouns where a single pronoun can refer to different antecedents across premises.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the example 'كلّ ما علمه العاقل فهو كما علمه والعاقل يعلم الحجر فهو كالحجر', the pronoun 'هو' is ambiguous between which two?
A) The rational and the stone.
B) The rational and the known.
C) The stone and the known.
D) The known and the unknown.

Key Takeaway
An ambiguous pronoun can change the meaning between premises and conclusion, invalidating the argument.

Fallacy of Ambiguous Conjunctive Particles
Third type: ambiguity of conjunctive particles (huruf al-nasq).

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify when the conjunction 'waw' causes a fallacy by conflating collection of parts and collection of attributes.

Core Idea
The same conjunction can mean 'and' in two different ways: combining parts or combining attributes.

The third type is the ambiguity of conjunctive particles, especially the letter 'waw'. The author gives the example 'five is even and odd.' This statement is true if we mean that five has the parts even and odd (e.g., 2+3), but false if we mean it is both even and odd as a single attribute. The word 'waw' can indicate collection of parts (like 'man is bone and flesh') or collection of attributes (like 'man is living and body'). The fallacy occurs when one interpretation is mistaken for the other.

Guided Path
1
Identify the conjunction
The conjunction 'waw' (and) is used. Note that it can have two functions: conjunction of parts or conjunction of attributes.
Both functions use the same word.
2
Distinguish the two meanings
Collection of parts means the subject has those parts; collection of attributes means the subject possesses those attributes simultaneously.
Example: 'man is bone and flesh' means man has those parts; 'man is living and body' means man is both living and a body.
3
Analyze the example
In 'five is even and odd,' if we take 'waw' as collection of parts, it is true because 5 is composed of even and odd numbers (e.g., 2+3). If taken as collection of attributes, it is false because 5 cannot be simultaneously even and odd as a single property.
The statement is true under one interpretation, false under the other.
4
Recognize the fallacy
One might think the statement 'five is even and odd' is true in an absolute sense, but it is only true under one reading. The ambiguity of 'waw' leads to a false inference.
The fallacy is in conflating the two meanings.

Source Text

Fallacy of Ambiguous Adjectives
Fourth type: ambiguity of adjectives.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify when an adjective is ambiguous between qualifying the subject or the predicate, causing a fallacy.

Core Idea
An adjective placed after a predicate may modify the subject or the predicate, leading to misinterpretation.

The fourth type is the ambiguity of adjectives. The author gives the example of saying 'Zayd is a physician, seeing' (Zaydun tabibun basirun). The adjective 'basir' (seeing) could be an attribute of the subject Zayd (meaning he is not blind) or an attribute of the predicate (meaning he is seeing in medicine). When these are combined, one might think Zayd is a seeing physician in the sense of being skilled in medicine. The fallacy arises because the adjective's role is unclear.

Guided Path
1
Identify the adjective
Find the adjective that comes after a noun and predicate. In the example, 'basir' (seeing) is the adjective.
The adjective is 'basir'.
2
Determine the possible references
The adjective could be describing the subject 'Zayd' (he is not blind) or describing the predicate 'physician' (he is seeing in medicine).
Two interpretations exist.
3
Examine the context
When the sentence is 'Zayd is a physician, seeing', the natural reading may be that Zayd is a physician and also seeing (not blind). But one might think it means he is a 'seeing physician' i.e., skilled.
The fallacy exploits the ambiguity.
4
Recognize the fallacy
If the argument relies on the mistaken interpretation, the conclusion may be false or unsupported.
The same wording can be true under one interpretation but false under another.

Sophistry and Fallacies
beginner

Verbal Obscurity: The Fallacy of False Premises
Distinguishes false premises from verbal vs conceptual confusion

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify verbal obscurity (iltibas al-lafz) as a cause of false premises and distinguish it from conceptual obscurity, using the examples provided.

Core Idea
Words that share core meaning but differ subtly can trick the mind into invalid inferences.

This passage identifies the fifth cause of fallacy: when a false premise is accepted due to either verbal or conceptual confusion. The author distinguishes between the two and provides examples of verbal obscurity. In this lesson, we focus on verbal obscurity: how subtle semantic differences between similar words can lead to logical errors if the mind overlooks the extra condition.

Guided Path
1
Identify the key terms in dispute
Look for pairs of words that share a core meaning but have a subtle difference. The passage gives examples: sitr/khidr, buka/'awil, thara/turab.
The extra condition might be something like 'enclosing a young girl' for khidr, 'raising voice' for 'awil, or 'moisture' for thara.
2
Determine the shared meaning and the extra condition
State the common core meaning (e.g., 'covering' for sitr/khidr) and the specific extra condition that distinguishes one from the other.
The extra condition is the key to why the words are not fully synonymous.
3
Check if a judgment is transferred from one term to the other
Observe if the argument assumes that because two words have the same core meaning, they are interchangeable. For example, if one knows a ruling about khidr, one might incorrectly apply it to sitr.
The fallacy occurs when the extra condition is forgotten.
4
Verify if the judgment holds when the extra condition is considered
Ask: does the judgment depend on the extra condition? If yes, then the transfer is invalid. The judgment about khidr (e.g., privacy requirements) may not apply to sitr if it lacks the condition.
This step catches the fallacy.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المثار الخامس : أن تكون المقدمة كاذبة وذلك لا يخلو إما أن يكون لالتباس اللفظ أو لالتباس المعنى، فإن لم يكن ثَمَّ شيء من هذه الأسباب لم يذعن الذهن له ولم يصدق به، فليس كلام إلا فيما يغلط فيه العقلاء. فأما من يصدق بكل ما يسمع فهو فاسد المزاج عسر العلاج أما التباس اللفظ فهو أن يكون بينه وبين الصادق مناسبة، كما إذا اشتركت لفظتان في معنى وبينهما افتراق في معنى دقيق، فيظن أن الحكم الذي ألفي صادقاً على أحدهما صادق على الآخر، ويقع الذهول عما فيه الافتراق من زيادة معنى أو نقصانه مع اتحاد المُسمّى، وذلك مما يكثر كلفظ الستر والخدر . ولا يقال : خدر إلَّا إذا كان مشتملاً على جارية، وإلا فهو ستر، وكالبكاء والعويل ولا يقال : عويل إلَّا إذا كان معه رفع صوت وإلا فهو بكاء، وقد يظن تساويهما، وكذا الثّرى والتراب فإن الثرى هو التراب ولكن بشرط
The fifth cause: that the premise is false. This is necessarily either due to verbal obscurity or due to conceptual obscurity. For if there is none of these reasons, the mind does not submit to it nor believe it. So there is no discussion except about that in which intelligent people err. As for the one who believes everything he hears, he is of corrupt temperament and difficult to treat. As for verbal obscurity, it is that there is an affinity between it and a true [statement], such as when two words share a meaning but have a difference in a subtle meaning, so one thinks that the judgment found true for one is true for the other, and one forgets the difference of increase or decrease in meaning despite the sameness of the named thing. This is frequent, like the word 'sitr' (curtain) and 'khidr' (veil). One does not say 'khidr' unless it encloses a young girl, otherwise it is 'sitr'. And like 'buka' (weeping) and '‘awil' (wailing): one does not say '‘awil' unless there is raising of voice, otherwise it is 'buka'. One may think they are equal. Similarly, 'thara' (damp earth) and 'turab' (dust): 'thara' is dust but with a condition.
Source pages p. 106

Commentary
This passage identifies the fifth cause of fallacy: a false premise that is accepted due to either verbal or conceptual confusion. The author states that if there is no such confusion, the mind simply rejects the false premise. He then defines verbal obscurity: two words share a core meaning but differ in a subtle additional condition, so one mistakenly transfers a judgment from one to the other. For example, 'sitr' (general curtain) and 'khidr' (veil that encloses a young girl) are not synonymous: 'khidr' has an extra condition. Similarly, 'buka' (weeping) and 'awil' (wailing with loud voice) differ only in the presence of loudness. Likewise, 'thara' (moist earth) is 'turab' (earth) with moisture. The fallacy occurs when the mind overlooks the extra condition, thinking the words are interchangeable. The lesson teaches that subtle semantic distinctions can lead to invalid inferences if ignored.

Key Terms
التباس اللفظ
التباس المعنى

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which of the following pairs of words illustrates a 'verbal obscurity' that could cause a fallacy?
A) 'buka' and '‘awil'
B) 'qalam' and 'kitab'
C) 'shams' and 'qamar'
D) 'rajul' and 'insan'

Key Takeaway
Verbal obscurity occurs when two words share a core meaning but differ in a subtle condition, causing a false premise if the condition is overlooked.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Consider the pair 'sitr' and 'khidr' from the text. Explain why judging a 'sitr' by the same rule as 'khidr' (e.g., requiring it to enclose a young girl) would be a fallacy. What extra condition distinguishes them?
?
Choose another pair of Arabic terms from Islamic sciences (e.g., 'qat'/'qatl khat' in fiqh of homicide, or 'salat'/'du'a'). Describe their shared meaning and the extra condition, and explain how confusing them could lead to a false premise.

Distinguishing Subject and Predicate with 'Who' Clauses
Author shows how 'alladhī' changes subject-predicate structure.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify whether a phrase introduced by alladhī belongs to the subject or the predicate in a proposition.

Core Idea
The relative pronoun alladhī attaches a phrase to the subject, not the predicate.

The placement of the relative pronoun 'who/which' (alladhī) in a Arabic proposition can change which part is the subject and which is the predicate. This lesson teaches you to parse such constructions correctly, a skill essential for precise logical analysis in Islamic sciences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the relative clause
Find the word 'alladhī' (who/which) in the proposition. It introduces a clause that qualifies the noun immediately before it.
2
Determine the referent of the clause
The clause after 'alladhī' refers back to the subject. Therefore, the entire phrase (subject + relative clause) acts as the subject of the main predicate.
3
Contrast with a simple conjunction
If the same phrase appears without 'alladhī' (e.g., as a second predicate), it becomes part of the predicate. Compare 'Zayd who is tall is white' with 'Zayd is tall, white'.
The presence of 'alladhī' keeps the descriptor in the subject; its absence moves it to the predicate.
4
Apply to a new example
Take a proposition from fiqh: 'The slaughterer who is a Muslim is permitted.' Here 'who is a Muslim' is part of the subject, so the ruling applies to Muslim slaughterers only.
If it said 'The slaughterer is a Muslim, permitted', it would be two separate predicates.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فالمحمول هو الأبيض فقط، والطويل من الموضوع، ويمكن أن يذكر الطويل بصيغة الذي فيرجع إلى زيد بأن تقول : زيد الذي هو طويل أبيض؛ وإن قلت: زيد طويل، أبيض، صار الطويل جزءاً من المحمول، وإذا لم يذكر الذي يكون بحيث يحتمل أن يراد به الذي وألا يراد كما تقول : الإنسانية من حيث هي إنسانية خاصة أو عامة، فيحتمل أن يكون الموضوع الإنسانية المجردة والمحمول الخاصة، ويحتمل أن يكون الموضوع الإنسانية فحسب والمحمول الخاصة من حيث هي إنسانية، إذا لو قلت : الإنسانية خاصة أو عامة لأخبرت عن شيء واحد. فإذا قلت : الإنسانية من حيث هي إنسانية خاصة أو عامة أخبرت عن شيئين وكل خبر فهو محمول. ولهذا لو قلت الإنسانية ليست من حيث هي إنسانية خاصة ولا عامة، صدق . ولو قلت : الإنسانية ليست خاصة ولا عامة كذب ويفهم الفرق بينهما عند ذكرنا المعنى الكلي في أحكام الوجود، فيتشعب من هذه التركيبات المختلفة أغاليط يعسر حلّها على حذاق النظار فضلاً عن الظاهريين، ولا تخلص عن مكامن الغلط إلا بتوفيق الله فليستوفق الله تعالى الناظر في هذه العقبات حتى يسلم عن ظلماتها .
So the predicate is only 'white', and the tall (al-tawīl) is part of the subject. It is possible to mention tall in the form of 'who' (alladhī), so it refers back to Zayd, as you say: 'Zayd who is tall is white.' And if you say: 'Zayd is tall, white,' then tall becomes part of the predicate. And when 'who' is not mentioned, it is such that it could possibly be intended to mean 'who' or not, as you say: 'Humanity insofar as it is humanity is particular or universal.' So it is possible that the subject is bare humanity and the predicate is 'particular', and it is possible that the subject is only humanity and the predicate is 'particular insofar as it is humanity'. For if you said: 'Humanity is particular or universal,' you would have reported about one thing. But if you say: 'Humanity insofar as it is humanity is particular or universal,' you have reported about two things, and every report is a predicate. For this reason, if you said: 'Humanity is not insofar as it is humanity particular nor universal,' it is true. But if you said: 'Humanity is not particular nor universal,' it is false. And the difference between them is understood when we mention the universal meaning in the judgments of existence. So from these different constructions, there branch off fallacies whose resolution is difficult even for skilled thinkers, let alone the exoterics. And there is no escape from the lurking places of error except through the success of God. So may God the Exalted grant success to the thinker in these obstacles so that he may be safe from their darkness.
Source pages p. 106

Commentary
This passage is a warning about a subtle logical error that arises from the way we construct subject-predicate propositions when we use relative clauses with 'الذي' (who/which). The author explains that the position of 'الذي' changes whether a word is part of the subject or part of the predicate. For example, in 'Zayd who is tall is white', the word 'tall' is part of the subject because it is attached to Zayd via 'who'. But in 'Zayd is tall, white', the word 'tall' becomes part of the predicate. This distinction is not always obvious. The author then gives a more complex example: 'Humanity insofar as it is humanity is particular or universal.' Without the 'من حيث هي' clause, this statement reports one attribute of humanity. But with it, it reports two: the subject is 'humanity' and the predicate is 'particular' with the qualification 'insofar as it is humanity'. The author shows that negating these two forms yields different truth values: negating the qualified form is true, while negating the unqualified form is false. This demonstrates how ambiguity can arise. The author concludes that such constructions can lead to fallacies that even skilled thinkers find hard to resolve. The only way to avoid them is through divine guidance. This passage teaches us to be precise in logical analysis, especially when dealing with relative clauses and qualifications in propositions.

Key Terms
الموضوع
المحمول
الذي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) In the predicate, because it describes Zayd.
B) In the subject, because it is attached via 'who'.
C) In both subject and predicate equally.

Key Takeaway
Relative pronoun 'alladhī' makes the following phrase part of the subject, not the predicate.

The Fallacy of Misleading Qualification in Propositions
Author warns of fallacies from ambiguous qualification in propositions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify fallacies arising from ambiguous qualification in subject-predicate structures and distinguish truth conditions.

Core Idea
A qualifying clause like 'insofar as' can create a compound predicate, making negation behave differently.

The author presents a deeper logical trap: when we add a qualification like 'insofar as it is humanity' to a proposition, we may be reporting about two things instead of one. This leads to different truth values and is a source of subtle fallacies. This lesson dissects that example.

Guided Path
1
Identify the qualifying phrase
Look for phrases like 'من حيث هي' (insofar as it is). This phrase adds a condition that affects the logical form.
2
Determine if the proposition is simple or compound
'Humanity is particular' reports one attribute. 'Humanity insofar as it is humanity is particular' reports two: the subject (humanity) and the predicate (particular under the condition).
The 'insofar as' introduces a second level of reporting.
3
Analyze the negation
Negate the qualified proposition: 'Humanity is not, insofar as it is humanity, particular' is true because it denies only the qualified attribute. But negating the simple proposition 'Humanity is not particular' is false.
The negation behaves differently because the scope includes the qualification.
4
Identify the potential fallacy
The fallacy occurs when one confuses the qualified proposition with the unqualified one, especially in arguments. For example, concluding that humanity is not particular based on the true qualified negation is invalid.
This is a fallacy of equivocation or scope fallacy.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Verbal Confusion: When Similar Words Deceive
Author explains verbal confusion with word pairs.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify instances of verbal confusion (iltibās al-lafẓ) in Arabic lexical pairs and explain how the subtle semantic difference leads to a fallacy.

Core Idea
Words that seem synonyms may differ in a subtle condition; mistaking them leads to false judgments.

The fifth cause of fallacy in syllogisms is a false premise. One major source is verbal confusion: words that share a core meaning but differ in a fine point. The author illustrates this with several Arabic word pairs, such as 'sitr' vs 'khidr', 'bukā'' vs 'ʿawīl', etc. In each case, a person may erroneously transfer a judgment from one word to the other because they overlook the subtle difference.

Guided Path
1
Identify the word pair
In the passage, find pairs of words that are similar but have a slight difference (e.g., sitr/khidr). Note the shared meaning and the distinguishing condition.
2
State the false inference
For each pair, explain what a person might wrongly conclude (e.g., 'khidr is just like sitr, so the same ruling applies').
3
Locate the subtle difference
Determine the extra condition that makes one word more specific (e.g., khidr requires a girl inside; otherwise it's just sitr).
4
Apply to an Islamic science example
Think of a similar lexical pair in fiqh or usul (e.g., ghasb and ightisāb?) and explain the danger of confusion.
For instance, 'talāq' (divorce) vs 'faskh' (annulment) have different preconditions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المثار الخامس : أن تكون المقدمة كاذبة وذلك لا يخلو إما أن يكون لالتباس اللفظ أو لالتباس المعنى، فإن لم يكن ثم شيء من هذه الأسباب لم يذعن الذهن له ولم يصدق به، فليس كلام إلا فيما يغلط فيه العقلاء. فأما من يصدق بكل ما يسمع فهو فاسد المزاج عسر العلاج. أما التباس اللفظ فهو أن يكون بينه وبين الصادق مناسبة، كما إذا اشتركت لفظتان في معنى وبينهما افتراق في معنى دقيق، فيظن أن الحكم الذي ألفي صادقاً على أحدهما صادق على الآخر، ويقع الذهول عما فيه الافتراق من زيادة معنى أو نقصانه مع اتحاد المُسمّى، وذلك مما يكثر كلفظ الستر والخدر . ولا يقال : خدر إلا إذا كان مشتملاً على جارية، وإلا فهو ستر، وكالبكاء والعويل ولا يقال : عويل إلا إذا كان معه رفع صوت وإلا فهو بكاء، وقد يظن تساويهما، وكذا الثرى والتراب فإن الثرى هو التراب ولكن بشرط النداوة، وكذلك المأزق والمضيق فإن المأزق هو المضيق ولكن لا يقال إلا في مواضع الحرب، وكذا الآبق والهارب فإن الآبق هو الهارب ولكن مع مزيد معنى في الهارب، وهو أن يكون من كد وخوف، فإن لم يكن سبب منفر فيسمى هارباً لا آبقاً؛ وكما لا يقال لماء الفم رضاب إلا ما دام في الفم فإذا فارقه فهو بزاق. ولا يقال للشجاع كمي إلا إذا كان شاكي السلاح وإلا فهو بطل. ولا يقال للشمس الغزالة إلا عند ارتفاع النهار ; فهذه الألفاظ متماثلة في الأصل وفيها نوع تفاوت، وقد يظن أن الحكم على أحدها حكم على الآخر فيصدق به لهذا السبب. وأما السبب المعنوي للتغليط فهو أن تكون المقدمة صادقة في البعض لا في الكل، فتؤخذ على أنها كلية وتصدق ويقع الذهول عن شرط صدقها، وأكثرها من سبق الوهم إلى العكس فإنا إذا قلنا : كل قود فعمد وكل رجم فبينا فيظن أن كل عمد ففيه قود وأن كل زنا ففيه رجم، وهذا كثير التغليط لمن لم يتحفظ عنه، والذي يصدق في البعض دون الكل قد يكون بحيث يصدق في بعض الموضوع كقولنا : الحيوان مكلف ; فإنه يصدق في الإنسان دون غيره، وقد يصدق في كل الموضوع ولكن في بعض الأحوال كقولنا : الإنسان مكلف، فإنه لا يصدق في حالة الصبا والجنون، وقد يصدق في بعض الأوقات كقولنا : المكلف يلزمه الصلاة، فإنه لا يصدق في وقت الضحى إذ لا يجب فيه صلاة، وقد يصدق بشرط خفي كقولنا : المكلف عليه شرب الخمر، فإنه بشرط ألا يكون مكرهاً فيترك يحرم الشرط ; وكذلك قولك : إذا قتل مظلوماً هو مثل من قتل; وهو صحيح بشرط، أعني ألا يكون القاتل أباً والقتيل ابناً. فهذه الأمور لما كانت تصدق في الأكثر ولا تنتهض كلية صادقة إلا إذا قيدت بالشرط، فربما يذعن الذهن للتصديق ويسلمها على أنها كلية صادقة فيلزم منها نتائج كاذبة.
The fifth cause: that the premise is false, and this is either due to verbal confusion (iltibās al-lafẓ) or due to conceptual confusion (iltibās al-maʿnā). If there is none of these causes, the mind does not assent to it and does not believe it, for speech concerns only what rational people err in. As for one who believes everything he hears, he is of corrupted temperament, difficult to treat. As for verbal confusion, it is that there is a resemblance between it and a true [statement]. For example, when two words share a meaning but have a subtle difference in meaning, one thinks that the judgment found true of one is true of the other, and one forgets the difference in terms of an increase or decrease of meaning despite the sameness of the referent. This is common, like the words 'sitr' (curtain) and 'khidr' (veil). One does not say 'khidr' unless it includes a girl, otherwise it is 'sitr'. And like 'bukā'' (crying) and 'ʿawīl' (wailing): one does not say 'ʿawīl' unless there is raising of the voice, otherwise it is 'bukā''. One may think they are equal. Similarly, 'tharā' (moist earth) and 'turāb' (dust): 'tharā' is dust but with the condition of moisture. And likewise 'maʾziq' (narrow pass) and 'mḍīq' (strait): 'maʾziq' is a strait but is only said in places of war. And similarly, 'ābiq' (fugitive) and 'hārib' (fleeing): 'ābiq' is the one who flees but with an additional meaning in the fleeing, which is that it is due to toil and fear. If there is no repellent cause, he is called 'hārib' not 'ābiq'. And just as one does not say of mouth water 'ruḍāb' except as long as it is in the mouth; when it leaves it, it is 'bizāq'. And one does not say of the brave man 'kamī' except if he is fully armed, otherwise he is 'baṭal'. And one does not say of the sun 'ghazāla' except at midday. So these words are similar in origin but have some variation, and one may think that the judgment on one is the judgment on the other, so one believes it for this reason. As for the conceptual cause of deception: it is that the premise is true in some cases but not all, so it is taken as universal and believed, and the condition of its truth is forgotten. Most of this comes from the mind jumping to the converse. Thus we say: 'Every retaliation (qawad) is intentional (ʿamd), and every stoning (rajm) is fornication (zinā)', then one thinks that every intentional act involves retaliation and every fornication involves stoning. This is a common deception for one who is not cautious about it. And that which is true in some but not all may be such that it is true for some of the subject, as our saying: 'Animal is responsible (mukallaf)'; it is true for humans but not others. Or it may be true for all the subject but in some states, as our saying: 'Human is responsible'; it is not true in the state of childhood or insanity. Or it may be true at some times, as our saying: 'The responsible person must pray'; it is not true at the time of forenoon (ḍuḥā) since no prayer is obligatory then. Or it may be true with a hidden condition, as our saying: 'The responsible person is subject to drinking wine'; it is with the condition that he is not coerced, otherwise the condition is violated. Similarly, your saying: 'If one is killed unjustly, it is like one who kills'; it is true with a condition, namely that the killer is not the father and the killed is not the son. When these matters are true in most cases but do not hold as universally true unless qualified by the condition, the mind may assent and accept them as universally true, leading to false conclusions.
Source pages p. 106

Commentary
This passage is an enumeration and explanation of the fifth cause of fallacy in syllogism: the false premise. The author divides this into two subtypes: verbal confusion and conceptual confusion. The verbal confusion occurs when two words share a core meaning but differ in a fine point, like 'sitr' and 'khidr' (both meaning cover, but khidr specifically refers to a cover that includes a girl). People erroneously transfer a judgment from one to the other. The author gives several Arabic examples to illustrate subtle differences: 'bukā'' vs 'ʿawīl', 'tharā' vs 'turāb', 'maʾziq' vs 'mḍīq', 'ābiq' vs 'hārib', 'ruḍāb' vs 'bizāq', 'kamī' vs 'baṭal', and 'ghazāla' vs 'shams'. Each pair has a common ground but one has an extra condition. The second subtype is conceptual confusion: a premise that is true in some cases but taken as universal. Examples include 'Every retaliation is intentional' – true, but the converse 'Every intentional act involves retaliation' is not always true (e.g., intentional killing without just cause may not be subject to retaliation if the killer is the father). The author also gives examples of premises true only for some subjects, some states, some times, or with hidden conditions, such as 'Animal is responsible' (only humans), 'Man is responsible' (not when insane), 'The responsible person must pray' (not at forenoon), and 'The responsible person is liable for drinking wine' (unless coerced). The mind tends to forget these conditions and treat the premises as universal, leading to fallacious conclusions.

Key Takeaway
Verbal confusion arises when words with a common meaning differ by a hidden condition, and the mind overlooks that condition.

Conceptual Confusion: When Partial Truths Become Universal
Author explains conceptual confusion with examples of hasty generalization and false converse.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify cases of conceptual confusion where a premise true in some instances is taken as universal, and recognize the role of the fallacy of the converse.

Core Idea
A premise true in part may be wrongly taken as universal, often by assuming the converse is also true.

The second subtype of false premise is conceptual confusion: a proposition that is true only for some subjects, states, times, or with hidden conditions is taken as universally true. The author gives examples like 'Every retaliation is intentional' leading to the false converse 'Every intentional act requires retaliation.' This fallacy often stems from a hasty conversion or ignoring necessary conditions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the universal claim being made
Find the premise that is being treated as universal (e.g., 'Every intentional act requires retaliation').
2
Check the original true statement
Locate the true statement from which it was derived (e.g., 'Every retaliation is intentional').
3
Notice the conversion error
Recognize that the universal affirmative ('All A are B') does not entail its converse ('All B are A').
4
Consider conditions or exceptions
For the examples like 'Animal is responsible', identify the condition (only humans) or state (not insane) or time (not at forenoon) or hidden condition (not coerced) that must be added.
5
Test with an Islamic example
Apply to a fiqh example: 'Every intoxicant is forbidden' does not imply 'Everything forbidden is intoxicant.'

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المثار الخامس : أن تكون المقدمة كاذبة وذلك لا يخلو إما أن يكون لالتباس اللفظ أو لالتباس المعنى، فإن لم يكن ثم شيء من هذه الأسباب لم يذعن الذهن له ولم يصدق به، فليس كلام إلا فيما يغلط فيه العقلاء. فأما من يصدق بكل ما يسمع فهو فاسد المزاج عسر العلاج. أما التباس اللفظ فهو أن يكون بينه وبين الصادق مناسبة، كما إذا اشتركت لفظتان في معنى وبينهما افتراق في معنى دقيق، فيظن أن الحكم الذي ألفي صادقاً على أحدهما صادق على الآخر، ويقع الذهول عما فيه الافتراق من زيادة معنى أو نقصانه مع اتحاد المُسمّى، وذلك مما يكثر كلفظ الستر والخدر . ولا يقال : خدر إلا إذا كان مشتملاً على جارية، وإلا فهو ستر، وكالبكاء والعويل ولا يقال : عويل إلا إذا كان معه رفع صوت وإلا فهو بكاء، وقد يظن تساويهما، وكذا الثرى والتراب فإن الثرى هو التراب ولكن بشرط النداوة، وكذلك المأزق والمضيق فإن المأزق هو المضيق ولكن لا يقال إلا في مواضع الحرب، وكذا الآبق والهارب فإن الآبق هو الهارب ولكن مع مزيد معنى في الهارب، وهو أن يكون من كد وخوف، فإن لم يكن سبب منفر فيسمى هارباً لا آبقاً؛ وكما لا يقال لماء الفم رضاب إلا ما دام في الفم فإذا فارقه فهو بزاق. ولا يقال للشجاع كمي إلا إذا كان شاكي السلاح وإلا فهو بطل. ولا يقال للشمس الغزالة إلا عند ارتفاع النهار ; فهذه الألفاظ متماثلة في الأصل وفيها نوع تفاوت، وقد يظن أن الحكم على أحدها حكم على الآخر فيصدق به لهذا السبب. وأما السبب المعنوي للتغليط فهو أن تكون المقدمة صادقة في البعض لا في الكل، فتؤخذ على أنها كلية وتصدق ويقع الذهول عن شرط صدقها، وأكثرها من سبق الوهم إلى العكس فإنا إذا قلنا : كل قود فعمد وكل رجم فبينا فيظن أن كل عمد ففيه قود وأن كل زنا ففيه رجم، وهذا كثير التغليط لمن لم يتحفظ عنه، والذي يصدق في البعض دون الكل قد يكون بحيث يصدق في بعض الموضوع كقولنا : الحيوان مكلف ; فإنه يصدق في الإنسان دون غيره، وقد يصدق في كل الموضوع ولكن في بعض الأحوال كقولنا : الإنسان مكلف، فإنه لا يصدق في حالة الصبا والجنون، وقد يصدق في بعض الأوقات كقولنا : المكلف يلزمه الصلاة، فإنه لا يصدق في وقت الضحى إذ لا يجب فيه صلاة، وقد يصدق بشرط خفي كقولنا : المكلف عليه شرب الخمر، فإنه بشرط ألا يكون مكرهاً فيترك يحرم الشرط ; وكذلك قولك : إذا قتل مظلوماً هو مثل من قتل; وهو صحيح بشرط، أعني ألا يكون القاتل أباً والقتيل ابناً. فهذه الأمور لما كانت تصدق في الأكثر ولا تنتهض كلية صادقة إلا إذا قيدت بالشرط، فربما يذعن الذهن للتصديق ويسلمها على أنها كلية صادقة فيلزم منها نتائج كاذبة.
The fifth cause: that the premise is false, and this is either due to verbal confusion (iltibās al-lafẓ) or due to conceptual confusion (iltibās al-maʿnā). If there is none of these causes, the mind does not assent to it and does not believe it, for speech concerns only what rational people err in. As for one who believes everything he hears, he is of corrupted temperament, difficult to treat. As for verbal confusion, it is that there is a resemblance between it and a true [statement]. For example, when two words share a meaning but have a subtle difference in meaning, one thinks that the judgment found true of one is true of the other, and one forgets the difference in terms of an increase or decrease of meaning despite the sameness of the referent. This is common, like the words 'sitr' (curtain) and 'khidr' (veil). One does not say 'khidr' unless it includes a girl, otherwise it is 'sitr'. And like 'bukā'' (crying) and 'ʿawīl' (wailing): one does not say 'ʿawīl' unless there is raising of the voice, otherwise it is 'bukā''. One may think they are equal. Similarly, 'tharā' (moist earth) and 'turāb' (dust): 'tharā' is dust but with the condition of moisture. And likewise 'maʾziq' (narrow pass) and 'mḍīq' (strait): 'maʾziq' is a strait but is only said in places of war. And similarly, 'ābiq' (fugitive) and 'hārib' (fleeing): 'ābiq' is the one who flees but with an additional meaning in the fleeing, which is that it is due to toil and fear. If there is no repellent cause, he is called 'hārib' not 'ābiq'. And just as one does not say of mouth water 'ruḍāb' except as long as it is in the mouth; when it leaves it, it is 'bizāq'. And one does not say of the brave man 'kamī' except if he is fully armed, otherwise he is 'baṭal'. And one does not say of the sun 'ghazāla' except at midday. So these words are similar in origin but have some variation, and one may think that the judgment on one is the judgment on the other, so one believes it for this reason. As for the conceptual cause of deception: it is that the premise is true in some cases but not all, so it is taken as universal and believed, and the condition of its truth is forgotten. Most of this comes from the mind jumping to the converse. Thus we say: 'Every retaliation (qawad) is intentional (ʿamd), and every stoning (rajm) is fornication (zinā)', then one thinks that every intentional act involves retaliation and every fornication involves stoning. This is a common deception for one who is not cautious about it. And that which is true in some but not all may be such that it is true for some of the subject, as our saying: 'Animal is responsible (mukallaf)'; it is true for humans but not others. Or it may be true for all the subject but in some states, as our saying: 'Human is responsible'; it is not true in the state of childhood or insanity. Or it may be true at some times, as our saying: 'The responsible person must pray'; it is not true at the time of forenoon (ḍuḥā) since no prayer is obligatory then. Or it may be true with a hidden condition, as our saying: 'The responsible person is subject to drinking wine'; it is with the condition that he is not coerced, otherwise the condition is violated. Similarly, your saying: 'If one is killed unjustly, it is like one who kills'; it is true with a condition, namely that the killer is not the father and the killed is not the son. When these matters are true in most cases but do not hold as universally true unless qualified by the condition, the mind may assent and accept them as universally true, leading to false conclusions.
Source pages p. 106

Commentary
This passage is an enumeration and explanation of the fifth cause of fallacy in syllogism: the false premise. The author divides this into two subtypes: verbal confusion and conceptual confusion. The verbal confusion occurs when two words share a core meaning but differ in a fine point, like 'sitr' and 'khidr' (both meaning cover, but khidr specifically refers to a cover that includes a girl). People erroneously transfer a judgment from one to the other. The author gives several Arabic examples to illustrate subtle differences: 'bukā'' vs 'ʿawīl', 'tharā' vs 'turāb', 'maʾziq' vs 'mḍīq', 'ābiq' vs 'hārib', 'ruḍāb' vs 'bizāq', 'kamī' vs 'baṭal', and 'ghazāla' vs 'shams'. Each pair has a common ground but one has an extra condition. The second subtype is conceptual confusion: a premise that is true in some cases but taken as universal. Examples include 'Every retaliation is intentional' – true, but the converse 'Every intentional act involves retaliation' is not always true (e.g., intentional killing without just cause may not be subject to retaliation if the killer is the father). The author also gives examples of premises true only for some subjects, some states, some times, or with hidden conditions, such as 'Animal is responsible' (only humans), 'Man is responsible' (not when insane), 'The responsible person must pray' (not at forenoon), and 'The responsible person is liable for drinking wine' (unless coerced). The mind tends to forget these conditions and treat the premises as universal, leading to fallacious conclusions.

العكس
المقدمة الكاذبة

Key Takeaway
Conceptual confusion occurs when a premise with implicit conditions is taken as unconditional, often through the fallacy of assuming the converse.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?
