Verbal Causes of Fallacy: When Words Mislead
Author explains verbal equivocation as a cause of fallacy in syllogism.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify when verbal similarity between terms leads to a fallacy in syllogistic reasoning.

Core Idea
Words similar in meaning often carry hidden conditions that affect their logical use.

In this passage, the author illustrates how words that seem synonymous can have subtle differences in application. The verbal cause of fallacy occurs when one mistakenly treats a ruling on one word as applying to another without checking its specific conditions. Let's see how this applies to logical arguments.

Guided Path
1
List the word pairs given
Identify the pairs: al-nadawa/al-madad, al-ma'zaq/al-madiq, al-abiq/al-harib, rudab/buzag, kami/batal, ghazala/shams.
These are examples from Arabic usage.
2
Extract the added condition for each
For each pair, note the specific context that restricts the second term. E.g., 'al-abiq' implies toil and fear, 'kami' requires full armor.
The condition is what makes the term apply only in certain situations.
3
Explain the fallacy
If one assumes the two words are fully synonymous, they may apply a ruling from one to the other incorrectly. This is a verbal fallacy.
The fallacy is in treating equivocal terms as univocal.
4
Generalize to syllogisms
When analyzing a syllogism, check that terms are used consistently and that no hidden conditions are being ignored.
This helps avoid false conclusions from verbal ambiguity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
النظر الثالث : في المغلظات في القياس
النداوة، وكذلك المأزق والمضيق فإن المأزق هو المضيق ولكن لا يقال إلا في مواضع الحرب، وكذا الآبق والهارب فإن الآبق هو الهارب ولكن مع مزيد معنى في الهارب، وهو أن يكون من كد وخوف، فإن لم يكن سبب منفر فيسمى هارباً لا ابقاً؛ وكما لا يقال لماء الفم رضاب إلا ما دام في الفم فإذا فارقه فهو بزاق. ولا يقال للشجاع كمي إلا إذا كان شاكي السلاح وإلا فهو بطل. ولا يقال للشمس الغزالة إلا عند ارتفاع النهار؛ فهذه الألفاظ متماثلة في الأصل وفيها نوع تفاوت، وقد يظنّ أن الحكم على أحدها حكم على الآخر فيصدق به لهذا السبب. وأما السبب المعنوي للتغليط فهو أن تكون المقدمة صادقة في البعض لا في الكلّ، فتؤخذ على أنها كلّية وتصدّق ويقع الذهول عن شرط صدقها، وأكثرها من سبق الوهم إلى العكس فإنَّا إذا قلنا : كل قود فبعمد وكل رجم فبزنا فيظن أن كلّ عمد ففيه قود وأن كل زنا ففيه رجم، وهذا كثير التغليط لمن لم يتحفظ عنه، والذي يصدق في البعض دون الكلّ قد يكون بحيث يصدق في بعض الموضوع كقولنا : الحيوان مكلف؛ فإنه يصدق في الإنسان دون غيره، وقد يصدق في كل الموضوع ولكن في بعض الأحوال كقولنا : الإنسان مكلّف، فإنه لا يصدق في حالة الصبا والجنون، وقد يصدق في بعض الأوقات كقولنا : المكلّف يلزمه الصلاة، فإنه لا يصدق في وقت الضحى إذ لا يجب فيه صلاة، وقد يصدق بشرط خفي كقولنا : المكلّف عليه شرب الخمر، فإنه بشرط ألا يكون مكرهاً فيترك يحرم الشرط؛ وكذلك قولك : إذا قتل مظلوماً هو مثل من قتل؛ وهو صحيح بشرط، أعني ألا يكون القاتل أباً والقتيل ابناً. فهذه الأمور لما كانت تصدق في الأكثر ولا تنتهض كلّية صادقة إلا إذا قيدت بالشرط، فربما يذعن الذهن للتصديق ويسلمها على أنها كلّية صادقة فيلزم منها نتائج كاذبة.
The third consideration: On fallacies in syllogism. Al-nadawa (humidity), and similarly al-ma'zaq (narrow pass) and al-madiq (strait) because al-ma'zaq is the narrow pass but is only said in places of war, and likewise al-abiq (fugitive) and al-harib (fleeing) because al-abiq is the fleeing one but with an added meaning in al-harib, namely that it is from toil and fear; if there is no repulsive reason, he is called harib not abiq. And just as water of the mouth is not called rudab except as long as it is in the mouth, when it leaves it is spittle. And the brave is not called kami except if he is fully armed, otherwise he is a hero. And the sun is not called ghazala except at midday; these words are similar in origin and have some difference. And it may be thought that the ruling on one is the ruling on the other, so it is affirmed for that reason. And as for the material cause of fallacy, it is that the premise is true in some cases not all, so it is taken as universal and believed, and the condition of its truth is forgotten; most of it comes from the prior assumption of the inverse. For when we say: Every qawad (retaliation) is by 'amd (intent) and every rajim (stoning) is by zina (adultery), it is thought that every 'amd involves qawad and every zina involves rajim. This is a frequent fallacy for one who is not guarded against it. What is true in some but not all may be true of some subject, as in our saying: 'Animal is obligated'; it is true of humans but not others. And it may be true of the whole subject but in some states, as in our saying: 'Humans are obligated'; it is not true in the state of childhood or insanity. And it may be true in some times, as in our saying: 'The obligated one must pray'; it is not true at forenoon because no prayer is obligatory. And it may be true with a hidden condition, as in our saying: 'The obligated one is required to drink wine' - it is with the condition that he is not coerced, otherwise the condition is violated. And similarly your saying: 'If one kills unjustly, he is like the one who killed' - it is correct with a condition, namely that the killer is not the father and the killed is not the son. Since these things are true in most cases and are not universally true unless qualified by the condition, the mind may incline to affirm them and accept them as universal truths, leading to false conclusions.
Source pages p. 107

Commentary
This passage explains two types of causes for fallacies in syllogism: verbal (لفظي) and material (معنوي). The verbal cause arises when words are similar in meaning but have nuanced differences, leading someone to think that a ruling on one applies to the other. The author gives examples: 'al-nadawa' is humidity but not exactly like general wetness; 'al-ma'zaq' is a narrow pass but specifically used in war contexts; 'al-abiq' is a fugitive but implies toil and fear; 'rudab' is saliva only while in the mouth; 'kami' is a brave warrior only when fully armed; 'ghazala' is the sun only at midday. These show that terms may be considered synonymous in origin but have specific conditions. The material cause is when a premise is true in some cases but is mistakenly taken as universal. This often comes from assuming the converse—for example, thinking that because every retaliation (qawad) involves intent ('amd), every intent involves retaliation, or because every stoning (rajim) involves adultery (zina), every adultery involves stoning. This is false because not every intent leads to retaliation, e.g., if the killer is the father. The author categorizes ways a premise can be true in some but not all: (1) true of only part of the subject, as 'animal is obligated' applies only to humans; (2) true of all the subject but only in certain states, e.g., 'human is obligated' except during childhood or insanity; (3) true only at certain times, e.g., 'the obligated one must pray' but not at forenoon when no prayer is obligatory; (4) true only with a hidden condition, e.g., 'the obligated one must drink wine' is conditioned on not being coerced, and 'if one kills unjustly he is like the one who killed' is conditioned on the killer not being the father of the victim. These partial truths, if accepted as universal without qualification, produce false conclusions. The passage emphasizes the need to identify conditions for universal validity.

Key Terms
التغليط
السبب اللفظي

D) all of the above

Key Takeaway
Verbal fallacies arise when subtle differences in word meaning are ignored.

Material Causes of Fallacy: When Partial Truths Are Taken as Universal
Author explains material cause of fallacy: taking a partial truth as universal.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify when a premise that is true only under certain conditions is mistakenly taken as universal, leading to a fallacy.

Core Idea
A premise true in many instances is not automatically universal; hidden conditions must be satisfied.

The second part of the passage explains the material cause of fallacy: a premise that is true in some cases but not all is taken as universal. The author gives examples from legal reasoning. We'll learn to detect when a premise is being overgeneralized.

Guided Path
1
Identify the four categories of partial truth
From the passage, note: (1) true of only part of the subject, (2) true of all subject but only in some states, (3) true only at some times, (4) true only with a hidden condition.
Each category corresponds to a type of condition.
2
Examine the example of the converse
The author gives 'Every qawad is by 'amd' and the mistaken assumption that every 'amd involves qawad. This is the fallacy of affirming the consequent.
The converse is not automatically valid.
3
Apply the categories to the examples
For instance, 'Human is obligated' is true of all humans but not in states of childhood or insanity (category 2).
Identify which category each example falls under.
4
Explain the consequence
If these premises are accepted as universal without qualification, they lead to false conclusions.
The mind may unconsciously assume the condition is met.

النظر الثالث : في المغلظات في القياس
النداوة، وكذلك المأزق والمضيق فإن المأزق هو المضيق ولكن لا يقال إلا في مواضع الحرب، وكذا الآبق والهارب فإن الآبق هو الهارب ولكن مع مزيد معنى في الهارب، وهو أن يكون من كد وخوف، فإن لم يكن سبب منفر فيسمى هارباً لا ابقاً؛ وكما لا يقال لماء الفم رضاب إلا ما دام في الفم فإذا فارقه فهو بزاق. ولا يقال للشجاع كمي إلا إذا كان شاكي السلاح وإلا فهو بطل. ولا يقال للشمس الغزالة إلا عند ارتفاع النهار؛ فهذه الألفاظ متماثلة في الأصل وفيها نوع تفاوت، وقد يظنّ أن الحكم على أحدها حكم على الآخر فيصدق به لهذا السبب. وأما السبب المعنوي للتغليط فهو أن تكون المقدمة صادقة في البعض لا في الكلّ، فتؤخذ على أنها كلّية وتصدّق ويقع الذهول عن شرط صدقها، وأكثرها من سبق الوهم إلى العكس فإنَّا إذا قلنا : كل قود فبعمد وكل رجم فبزنا فيظن أن كلّ عمد ففيه قود وأن كل زنا ففيه رجم، وهذا كثير التغليط لمن لم يتحفظ عنه، والذي يصدق في البعض دون الكلّ قد يكون بحيث يصدق في بعض الموضوع كقولنا : الحيوان مكلف؛ فإنه يصدق في الإنسان دون غيره، وقد يصدق في كل الموضوع ولكن في بعض الأحوال كقولنا : الإنسان مكلّف، فإنه لا يصدق في حالة الصبا والجنون، وقد يصدق في بعض الأوقات كقولنا : المكلّف يلزمه الصلاة، فإنه لا يصدق في وقت الضحى إذ لا يجب فيه صلاة، وقد يصدق بشرط خفي كقولنا : المكلّف عليه شرب الخمر، فإنه بشرط ألا يكون مكرهاً فيترك يحرم الشرط؛ وكذلك قولك : إذا قتل مظلوماً هو مثل من قتل؛ وهو صحيح بشرط، أعني ألا يكون القاتل أباً والقتيل ابناً. فهذه الأمور لما كانت تصدق في الأكثر ولا تنتهض كلّية صادقة إلا إذا قيدت بالشرط، فربما يذعن الذهن للتصديق ويسلمها على أنها كلّية صادقة فيلزم منها نتائج كاذبة.
The third consideration: On fallacies in syllogism. Al-nadawa (humidity), and similarly al-ma'zaq (narrow pass) and al-madiq (strait) because al-ma'zaq is the narrow pass but is only said in places of war, and likewise al-abiq (fugitive) and al-harib (fleeing) because al-abiq is the fleeing one but with an added meaning in al-harib, namely that it is from toil and fear; if there is no repulsive reason, he is called harib not abiq. And just as water of the mouth is not called rudab except as long as it is in the mouth, when it leaves it is spittle. And the brave is not called kami except if he is fully armed, otherwise he is a hero. And the sun is not called ghazala except at midday; these words are similar in origin and have some difference. And it may be thought that the ruling on one is the ruling on the other, so it is affirmed for that reason. And as for the material cause of fallacy, it is that the premise is true in some cases not all, so it is taken as universal and believed, and the condition of its truth is forgotten; most of it comes from the prior assumption of the inverse. For when we say: Every qawad (retaliation) is by 'amd (intent) and every rajim (stoning) is by zina (adultery), it is thought that every 'amd involves qawad and every zina involves rajim. This is a frequent fallacy for one who is not guarded against it. What is true in some but not all may be true of some subject, as in our saying: 'Animal is obligated'; it is true of humans but not others. And it may be true of the whole subject but in some states, as in our saying: 'Humans are obligated'; it is not true in the state of childhood or insanity. And it may be true in some times, as in our saying: 'The obligated one must pray'; it is not true at forenoon because no prayer is obligatory. And it may be true with a hidden condition, as in our saying: 'The obligated one is required to drink wine' - it is with the condition that he is not coerced, otherwise the condition is violated. And similarly your saying: 'If one kills unjustly, he is like the one who killed' - it is correct with a condition, namely that the killer is not the father and the killed is not the son. Since these things are true in most cases and are not universally true unless qualified by the condition, the mind may incline to affirm them and accept them as universal truths, leading to false conclusions.
Source pages p. 107

Commentary
This passage explains two types of causes for fallacies in syllogism: verbal (لفظي) and material (معنوي). The verbal cause arises when words are similar in meaning but have nuanced differences, leading someone to think that a ruling on one applies to the other. The author gives examples: 'al-nadawa' is humidity but not exactly like general wetness; 'al-ma'zaq' is a narrow pass but specifically used in war contexts; 'al-abiq' is a fugitive but implies toil and fear; 'rudab' is saliva only while in the mouth; 'kami' is a brave warrior only when fully armed; 'ghazala' is the sun only at midday. These show that terms may be considered synonymous in origin but have specific conditions. The material cause is when a premise is true in some cases but is mistakenly taken as universal. This often comes from assuming the converse—for example, thinking that because every retaliation (qawad) involves intent ('amd), every intent involves retaliation, or because every stoning (rajim) involves adultery (zina), every adultery involves stoning. This is false because not every intent leads to retaliation, e.g., if the killer is the father. The author categorizes ways a premise can be true in some but not all: (1) true of only part of the subject, as 'animal is obligated' applies only to humans; (2) true of all the subject but only in certain states, e.g., 'human is obligated' except during childhood or insanity; (3) true only at certain times, e.g., 'the obligated one must pray' but not at forenoon when no prayer is obligatory; (4) true only with a hidden condition, e.g., 'the obligated one must drink wine' is conditioned on not being coerced, and 'if one kills unjustly he is like the one who killed' is conditioned on the killer not being the father of the victim. These partial truths, if accepted as universal without qualification, produce false conclusions. The passage emphasizes the need to identify conditions for universal validity.

Key Terms
السبب المعنوي
العكس

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which of the following is an example of a premise true in all subjects but only in some states?
A) 'Animal is obligated'
B) 'Human is obligated'
C) 'The obligated one must pray'
D) 'The obligated one is required to drink wine'

Key Takeaway
Material fallacies occur when conditions of truth are forgotten, leading to universalizing a partial truth.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Take the fiqh statement 'Every intoxicant is forbidden.' List one condition under which this premise might not hold (e.g., small amount not intoxicating) and explain how ignoring this condition could lead to a fallacy.

Sixth Cause: Begging the Question (المصادرة على المطلوب)
Author defines and illustrates begging the question with examples from fiqh.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify when an argument commits the fallacy of begging the question in Islamic legal reasoning.

Core Idea
Begging the question: assuming what you need to prove.

The author now turns to the sixth type of fallacy in syllogism: musadara 'ala al-matlub (begging the question). This occurs when the premises already contain the conclusion, so the argument offers no genuine proof. He illustrates with two fiqh arguments—one about women contracting marriage, another about voluntary fasting. Understanding this fallacy is crucial for distinguishing valid demonstrations from circular reasoning in Islamic sciences.

Guided Path
1
Understand the definition
The author says 'the premises are not distinct from the conclusion' – that is, the conclusion is already assumed in one of the premises. Recognize that this makes the argument circular.
2
Analyze the first example
'A woman is under guardianship, so she may not contract marriage.' Ask: What does 'under guardianship' mean? If the only meaning is that she cannot contract marriage independently, then the premise is just restating the conclusion.
This shows how a term can be defined in a way that presupposes the point in dispute.
3
Analyze the second example
'Voluntary fasting is valid with daytime intention because it is a specific fast (sawm 'ayn).' The disputants try to define 'specific fast' but end up including the very point at issue (suitability for voluntary fasting).
The cause must be independent of the judgment; here the judgment is made part of the cause.
4
Identify the pattern
In both examples, the 'illa (cause) is not independent of the hukm (judgment). The author emphasizes that the cause must be constituted separately from the judgment that follows from it.
This applies to any argument: the premise should provide new evidence, not merely restate the conclusion.
5
Apply the test
For any argument, ask: Does the premise add new information or merely repeat the conclusion? If repeated, it is begging the question. Practise by evaluating claims in fiqh or kalam debates.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المثار السادس : أن لا تكون المقدمات غير النتيجة فتصادر على المطلوب في المقدمات من حيث لا تدري، كقولك : إن المرأة مولى عليها فلا تلي عقد النكاح، وإذا طُولبت بمعنى كونها مولى عليها ربما لم تتمكن من إظهار معنى سوى ما فيه النزاع. وكذلك قول القائل : يصح التطوّع بنية تنشأ نهاراً لأنه صوم عين، وإذا طولب بتحقيق معنى كونه صوم عين لم يستغن عن أن يجعل النتيجة جزءاً منه، إذ يقال له: ما معنى كونه صوم عين؟ فيقول : إنه يصلح للتطوع . فيقال : وبهذا لا يثبت التعين إذ يصلح كلّ يوم قبل طلوع الفجر للقضاء، ولا يقال : صوم عين. وإن قال : معناه أنه لا يصلح لغير التطوع، يقال : وبهذا لا يثبت التعين فإن الليل لا يصلح لغير التطوّع، ولا يقال له : عين، فيضطر إلى أن يجمع بين المعنيين ويقول : معناه أنه يصلح للتطوع ولا يصلح لغيره فيقال : قوله : يصلح للتطوع هو الحكم المطلوب، علمه فكيف جعله جزءاً من العلة والعلة ينبغي أن تتقوم ذاتها دون الحكم؟ ثم يترتب عليها الحكم فيكون الحكم غير العلة، ونظائر هذا في العقليات تكثر فلذلك لم نذكره.
The sixth cause: That the premises are not distinct from the conclusion, so one begs the question in the premises without realizing it. For example, your saying: 'A woman is under guardianship, so she may not contract marriage.' If asked for the meaning of her being under guardianship, perhaps you cannot give a meaning other than the matter in dispute. Likewise one's saying: 'Voluntary fasting is valid with an intention made during the day because it is a specific fast (sawm 'ayn).' If asked to verify the meaning of it being a specific fast, he cannot avoid making the conclusion part of it. For it is said to him: What is the meaning of its being a specific fast? He says: It is suitable for voluntary fasting. It is said: By this, specificity is not established, for every day before dawn is suitable for making up missed fasts, and that is not called a specific fast. If he says: Its meaning is that it is not suitable for other than voluntary fasting, it is said: By this, specificity is not established, for the night is not suitable for other than voluntary fasting, and that is not called a specific fast. So he is forced to combine the two meanings and say: Its meaning is that it is suitable for voluntary fasting and not suitable for other than it. Then it is said: His statement 'it is suitable for voluntary fasting' is the very judgment sought; how can he make it part of the cause? The cause should be constituted independently of the judgment, then the judgment follows from it, so the judgment is distinct from the cause. Such examples are many in rational matters, so we did not mention them.
Source pages p. 107

Commentary
This passage defines the sixth cause of fallacy in syllogism: begging the question (musadara 'ala al-matlub), where the conclusion is already presupposed in the premises. The author gives two fiqh examples. First: 'A woman is under guardianship (muwallā 'alayhā), so she may not contract marriage.' If challenged to explain what 'under guardianship' means, the only available meaning is the very issue in dispute—that she cannot marry independently. Thus the argument is circular. Second: A debate about voluntary fasting: someone claims that a voluntary fast is valid if the intention is made during the day because it is a 'specific fast' (sawm 'ayn). When asked to define 'specific fast,' he first says 'it is suitable for voluntary fasting,' but that is exactly the point to be proved. Then he says 'it is not suitable for other than voluntary fasting,' but that is also the same judgment (since 'not suitable for other than voluntary' = only suitable for voluntary). The opponent points out that the night also is not suitable for other than voluntary fasting, yet it is not called a specific fast. Finally he combines: 'suitable for voluntary and not suitable for other,' but the first part is the judgment itself. The author explains that the cause ('illa) must be independent of the judgment; here the judgment is made part of the cause, which is invalid. The passage highlights the fallacy of circular reasoning in argumentation.

Key Terms
المصادرة على المطلوب
صوم العين
العلة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Because it is not true that voluntary fasting requires intention during the day.
B) Because 'suitable for voluntary fasting' is the very judgment to be proved, not a separate cause.
C) Because 'specific fast' means something else entirely.
D) Because voluntary fasting is invalid without night intention.

Key Takeaway
Begging the question occurs when the premise presupposes the conclusion, offering no real proof; the cause must be independent of the judgment.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Consider the argument: 'Riba is forbidden because it involves excess (fadl) without a valid countervalue.' If someone asks 'What is excess?' and you answer 'Excess is the extra given in a riba transaction,' is this begging the question? Explain why or why not.

The Principle of Epistemic Priority: Premises Must Be More Known
Author discusses fallacies when premises are equally or less known.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify arguments where premises are equal in knowledge to the conclusion and explain why they are fallacious.

Core Idea
Premises must be more known than the conclusion; correlatives cannot prove each other.

This passage introduces the seventh cause of fallacy in demonstration: when the premises are not more known than the conclusion. You will learn to spot arguments that commit this error, especially those using correlatives.

Guided Path
1
Identify conclusion and premises
Find the claim being proved and the evidence given. In the example, the conclusion is that Zayd is the son of 'Amr, and the premise is that 'Amr is the father of Zayd.
2
Check epistemic parity
Determine if the premise is as well-known as the conclusion. Correlatives like father-son are known together; neither is prior.
3
Recognize the correlatives pattern
Notice when each term's meaning depends on the other. The premise uses one correlative to prove the other.
4
Explain the fallacy
Since correlatives are equally known, the premise cannot be more known than the conclusion, violating the requirement for demonstration.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المثار السابع : أن لا تكون المقدمات أعرف من النتيجة، بل تكون إما مساوية لها في المعرفة كالمتضايفات، وذلك مثل من ينازع في كون زيد ابناً لعمرو فيقول : الدليل على أن زيداً ابن لعمرو هو أن عمراً أباً لزيد، وهذا محال لأنهما يعلمان معاً ولا يعلم أحدهما بالآخر، وكذلك من يثبت أن وصفاً من الأوصاف علم بقوله : الدليل عليه أن المحل الذي قام به عالم وهو هوس إذ لا يعلم كون المحل عالماً إلا مع العلم بكون الحال في المحل علماً. وقد تكون المقدمة متأخرة في المعرفة عن النتيجة فيكون قياساً دورياً، وأمثلته في العقليات كثيرة. وأما في الفقهيات فكأن يقول الحنفي : تبطل صلاة المتيمم إذا وجد الماء في خلالها لأنه قدر على الاستعمال وكلّ من قدر على استعمال الماء لزمه، ومن يلزمه استعمال الماء فلا يجوز له أن يصلي بالتيمم فيجعل القدرة على الاستعمال حدا أوسط وبطلان الصلاة نتيجة فيقال : إن أردت به القدرة حساً فيبطل بما لو وجده مملوكاً
The seventh cause: That the premises are not more known than the conclusion, but rather are either equal to it in knowledge, like correlatives. For example, one who disputes that Zayd is the son of 'Amr says: The proof that Zayd is the son of 'Amr is that 'Amr is the father of Zayd. This is impossible because they are known together; one is not known through the other. Likewise, one who establishes that a certain attribute is knowledge by saying: The proof is that the substrate in which it inheres is knowing—this is nonsense, because the substrate being knowing is not known except along with the knowledge inhering in it. And sometimes the premise is posterior in knowledge to the conclusion, resulting in a circular syllogism; examples of this are many in rational matters. As for juridical matters, consider the Hanafi's saying: 'The prayer of one who performs tayammum is invalidated if he finds water during it because he has the ability to use it, and everyone who has the ability to use water must use it, and one who must use water is not permitted to pray with tayammum'—making the ability to use water the middle term and the invalidation of prayer the conclusion. It is said: If you mean the ability in a sensory sense, it is refuted by the case where he finds water that is owned (not free to use).
Source pages p. 108

Commentary
This passage identifies the seventh cause of fallacy: when the premises are not more known (a'raf) than the conclusion. The author explains two subtypes: (1) premises equal in knowledge to the conclusion, as in correlatives (mutadāyifāt), e.g., trying to prove Zayd is 'Amr's son by stating 'Amr is Zayd's father—both are known together, so neither proves the other. Another example: proving that a certain attribute is knowledge by saying the subject is knowing, but that presupposes the attribute is knowledge. (2) Premises posterior to the conclusion in knowledge, leading to a circular syllogism (qiyās dawrī). The author then gives a fiqh example from the Hanafi school: the claim that a person performing tayammum invalidates his prayer if he finds water during it, because he has the ability to use water, and whoever has the ability must use it, so he cannot pray with tayammum. The fallacy is that 'ability to use water' is ambiguous: if it means sensory ability (the water is physically present), the argument fails because finding owned water (e.g., in someone else's property) does not give one the right to use it. Thus the premise is not more known; it requires qualification. The passage highlights the importance of epistemic priority in demonstration.

Key Terms
المتضايفات
القياس الدورى
الحد الأوسط

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the correlatives fallacy example from the passage, why does the argument that Zayd is the son of 'Amr because 'Amr is the father of Zayd fail?
A) Because the conclusion is false.
B) Because 'father' and 'son' are correlatives known together; neither is more known.
C) Because the premise uses a different relationship.
D) Because the argument is circular, not merely equal.

Key Takeaway
Premises in a demonstration must be more known than the conclusion; using correlatives violates this rule.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Identify the correlatives fallacy in the following argument: 'This act is obligatory because it is not prohibited.' Explain why it fails.

Circular Syllogism: The Hanafi Tayammum Example
Author presents a circular syllogism from Hanafi fiqh.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to detect circular reasoning in a syllogism where the middle term is ambiguous and not more known than the conclusion.

Core Idea
A syllogism is circular if the middle term is not more known than the conclusion, often due to ambiguity.

This lesson focuses on the second subtype of the seventh cause: circular syllogism. You will analyze a Hanafi juridical example involving tayammum and see how ambiguity in the middle term makes the argument fallacious.

Guided Path
1
Extract the syllogism
The Hanafi argument: Major premise: Everyone who has the ability to use water must use it. Minor premise: The person performing tayammum has the ability to use water (because he found water). Conclusion: Therefore, he is not permitted to pray with tayammum (i.e., his prayer is invalidated).
The middle term is 'ability to use water' (al-qudra 'ala al-isti'mal).
2
Check the middle term's epistemic status
Is 'ability to use water' more known than the conclusion? The author points out it is ambiguous: sensory ability (finding water) vs. legal ability (right to use). The conclusion hinges on this ambiguity.
3
Identify the ambiguity
If 'ability' means sensory presence, the minor premise is true but insufficient to force the conclusion (owned water cannot be used). If it means legal ability, the minor premise is not established (finding water does not grant permission if owned).
4
Explain the circularity
The middle term is not more known than the conclusion; in fact, knowing whether the ability is legal requires knowing the ruling on tayammum, making the argument circular.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المثار السابع : أن لا تكون المقدمات أعرف من النتيجة، بل تكون إما مساوية لها في المعرفة كالمتضايفات، وذلك مثل من ينازع في كون زيد ابناً لعمرو فيقول : الدليل على أن زيداً ابن لعمرو هو أن عمراً أباً لزيد، وهذا محال لأنهما يعلمان معاً ولا يعلم أحدهما بالآخر، وكذلك من يثبت أن وصفاً من الأوصاف علم بقوله : الدليل عليه أن المحل الذي قام به عالم وهو هوس إذ لا يعلم كون المحل عالماً إلا مع العلم بكون الحال في المحل علماً. وقد تكون المقدمة متأخرة في المعرفة عن النتيجة فيكون قياساً دورياً، وأمثلته في العقليات كثيرة. وأما في الفقهيات فكأن يقول الحنفي : تبطل صلاة المتيمم إذا وجد الماء في خلالها لأنه قدر على الاستعمال وكلّ من قدر على استعمال الماء لزمه، ومن يلزمه استعمال الماء فلا يجوز له أن يصلي بالتيمم فيجعل القدرة على الاستعمال حدا أوسط وبطلان الصلاة نتيجة فيقال : إن أردت به القدرة حساً فيبطل بما لو وجده مملوكاً

For example, one who disputes that Zayd is the son of 'Amr says: The proof that Zayd is the son of 'Amr is that 'Amr is the father of Zayd. It is said: If you mean the ability in a sensory sense, it is refuted by the case where he finds water that is owned (not free to use).
Source pages p. 108

This passage identifies the seventh cause of fallacy: when the premises are not more known (a'raf) than the conclusion. The author explains two subtypes: (1) premises equal in knowledge to the conclusion, as in correlatives (mutadāyifāt), e.g., trying to prove Zayd is 'Amr's son by stating 'Amr is Zayd's father—both are known together, so neither proves the other. On Sophistry and the Causes of Disagreement
Author explains why sophists deny knowledge and why disagreements occur in intellectual matters.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the author's two-fold answer regarding the sources of sophistry and intellectual disagreement, and differentiate between the two types of sophists described.

Core Idea
Sophistry arises not from logic's failure but from intellectual deficiency and the conflict between reason and estimation.

You might wonder: if the logical method is so clear, why do some people deny all certain knowledge? And why do intelligent scholars disagree so fiercely in theology and law? This passage tackles these questions head-on. The author gives a nuanced answer: the problem is not with logic itself, but with how human minds—especially the faculty of estimation (wahm)—relate to abstract conclusions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the challenge
Find the rhetorical question the author raises: if premises are necessary and true, and the syllogistic form is clear, then why do sophists deny knowledge and claim equipollence of proofs (takāfuʾ al-adilla)?
2
Extract the first cause of disagreement
Note the author's first answer: most intellects are deficient in grasping the conditions of valid proof. Moreover, intellectual proofs often lead to conclusions that the estimation (wahm) rejects, unlike mathematical proofs where reason and estimation cooperate.
The author compares intellectual sciences to mathematical ones: in math, errors are quickly recognized and corrected; in intellectual sciences, errors persist because the conclusions counter ingrained imaginative assumptions.
3
Analyze the difference between the two types of sophists
Identify two categories: (1) those who deny even primary necessary truths (like 'two is more than one' or one's own existence) due to bad temperament and mental corruption from excessive theoretical perplexity; (2) those who accept necessary truths but claim that proofs are equipollent in theoretical matters, driven by observing contradictions among the arguments of different theological schools.
The first type suffers from a kind of philosophical madness; the second type is misled by apparently equal opposing arguments.
4
Evaluate the author's diagnosis
Consider whether the author's explanation is convincing. Does it apply to modern forms of skepticism? How does understanding these causes help us respond to those who deny the possibility of knowledge?
This step connects the passage to broader epistemological concerns.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفصل الثاني في بيان خيال السوفسطائية : فإن قال قائل : إذا كانت المقدمات ضرورية صادقة، والعقول مشتملة عليها وهذا الترتيب الذي ذكرتموه في صورة القياس أيضاً واضح فمن أين وقع للسوفسطائية إنكار العلوم والقول بتكافؤ الأدلة؟ أو من أين ثارت الاختلافات بين الناس في المعقولات؟ قلنا: أما وقوع الخلاف فلقصور أكثر الأفهام عن الشروط التي ذكرناها ومن يتأملها لم يتعجب من المخالف فيها، لا سيما وأدلة العقول تنساق إلى نتائج لا يذعن الوهم لها، بل يكذب بها لا كالعلوم الحسابية فإن الوهم والعقل يتعاونان فيها، ثم من لا يعرف الأمور الحسابية يعرف أنه لا يعرفها وإن غلط، فلا يدوم غلطه بل يمكن إزالته على القرب. وأما العلوم العقلية فليس كذلك ؛ ثم من السوفسطائية من أنكر العلوم الأولية والحسية، كعلمنا بأن الاثنين أكثر من الواحد وكعلمنا بوجودنا، وأن الشيء الواحد إما أن يكون قديماً أو حادثاً، فهؤلاء دخلهم الخلل من سوء المزاج وفساد الذهن بكثرة التحيّر في النظريات وأما الذين سلموا الضروريات وزعموا أن الأدلة متكافئة في النظريات فإنما حملهم عليه ما رأوا من تناقض أدلة فرق المتكلمين
The second chapter: explaining the illusion of the sophists. For if it is said: if the premises are necessary and true, and minds encompass them, and this arrangement that you mentioned in the form of the syllogism is also clear, then where did the sophists get the denial of sciences and the claim of the equipollence of proofs? Or from where did disagreements among people arise in intellectual matters? We say: as for the occurrence of disagreement, it is due to the deficiency of most intellects concerning the conditions we mentioned. Whosoever contemplates them will not be surprised at opposition in them, especially since intellectual proofs lead to results that the estimation (wahm) does not acquiesce to, but rather denies, unlike the mathematical sciences, for in them estimation and intellect cooperate. Then, whoever does not know mathematical matters knows that he does not know them, and if he errs, his error does not persist but can be removed in short order. But intellectual sciences are not like that. Then, among the sophists, some denied the primary and sensory sciences, such as our knowledge that two is more than one, and our knowledge of our own existence, and that one thing is either eternal or originated. These people were afflicted by bad temperament and corruption of the mind due to much perplexity in theoretical matters. As for those who accepted the necessary truths and claimed that proofs are equipollent in theoretical matters, they were driven by what they saw of the contradiction among the proofs of the different schools of the mutakallimun.
Source pages p. 109

Commentary
This passage addresses a challenge: if the logical method outlined is sound, why do sophists deny knowledge entirely, and why do people disagree in intellectual matters? The author gives a twofold answer. First, disagreement arises from intellectual deficiency in grasping the necessary conditions for valid reasoning. Intellectual proofs often yield conclusions that the faculty of estimation (wahm) rejects, unlike mathematics where estimation and reason cooperate. In mathematics, errors are short-lived and easily corrected. But in intellectual sciences, errors persist because the conclusions counter ingrained imaginative assumptions. Second, the author categorizes sophists into two types. The first type denies primary necessary truths (like 'two is more than one') and sensory facts (like one's own existence) – such sophists suffer from a mental disorder or a corrupted temperament due to excessive confusion in theoretical matters. The second type accepts necessary truths but claims that proofs are equipollent in theoretical matters, i.e., that arguments for opposing positions are equally strong. This stance arises from seeing the contradictory arguments among different theological schools (mutakallimun), which leads them to doubt that any proof is decisive. The author implies that such contradiction is not inherent in logic but results from misuse or misunderstanding of the conditions of valid proof.

Key Terms
السوفسطائية
تكافؤ الأدلة
الوهم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) They have not studied logic properly.
B) They suffer from bad temperament and mental corruption due to excessive theoretical perplexity.
C) They adhere to a false philosophical school.
D) They reject the authority of the mutakallimun.

Key Takeaway
Disagreement in intellectual matters arises from deficiency in grasping logical conditions and from the tension between reason and estimation, not from any defect in logic itself.

The Fallacy of Hasty Generalization from Rhetorical Premises
Author analyzes the first fallacy: syllogism from rhetorical premises.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify arguments that commit hasty generalization by using rhetorical premises and distinguish them from valid inductive reasoning in Islamic sciences.

Core Idea
One example never proves a universal; even many only yield probability.

In this passage, the author analyzes a common logical fallacy: taking a single case and making a universal judgment. He explains that such arguments are built on rhetorical, not demonstrative premises, and warns that even many examples only produce opinion (zann), never certain knowledge (ilm). This is crucial for evaluating arguments in Kalam and Fiqh, where hasty generalizations can lead to false theological or legal conclusions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the type of premises
Look for language that is rhetorical (وعظية خطابية) rather than demonstrative. These premises often use stories, analogies, or emotional appeals.
In Islamic texts, rhetorical premises are common in sermons (khutba) but are not suitable for certain knowledge.
2
Find the universal claim
Determine what is being asserted about all members of a category. The fallacy is that the claimant judges a universal (جميع) based on one or a few instances.
Example: 'One heavy object falls, so all heavy objects fall.'
3
Count the instances used
Note how many particulars (جزئيات) are cited. The author says even many are insufficient for certainty, so one is especially weak.
The mere number does not transform probability into certainty.
4
Apply the author's epistemic rule
The passage states: judging all by many particulars is impossible (ممتنع), so how can it be by one? The best you can achieve is opinion (ظن), which increases with more examples but never reaches science (علم).
This rule guards against overconfidence in inductive arguments.
5
Test with an Islamic example
Take a real case: a student argues 'One traveler shortened his prayer, so all travelers must shorten prayer.' Identify why this is a hasty generalization.
The correct method requires examining the 'illah (cause) and textual evidence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فأما الأول : من هذه الأمثلة : فهو قياس ألّف من مقدمات وعظية خطابية، إذ أخذ فيه شيء واحد ووجد على وجه فحكم به على الجميع. ونحن قد بينا أن الحكم على الجميع بجزئيات كثيرة ممتنع فكيف الحكم بجزئي واحد، بل إذا كثرت الجزئيات لم تفد إلّا الظنّ، ثم لا يزال يزداد الظنّ قوة بكثرة الأمثلة ولكن لا ينتهي إلى العلم.
As for the first of these examples: It is a syllogism composed of homiletic, rhetorical premises, because one thing was taken, found in one aspect, and then judged upon all. And we have already shown that judging all by many particulars is impossible, so how can it be by a single particular? Rather, if the particulars are many, they yield only opinion, and then opinion continues to increase in strength with many examples, but it does not reach knowledge.
Source pages p. 113

Commentary
This passage analyzes the first example from the list of fallacies. The author states that this syllogism is built from rhetorical (وعظية خطابية) premises. The fallacy is that it takes a single instance and makes a universal judgment. For example, if one observes that some heavy objects fall, one might conclude that all heavy objects fall, but this is not certain. The author emphasizes that judging a universal by many particulars is impossible, so how can it be done by one? Even many particulars only yield opinion (ظن), never certain knowledge (علم). This guards against hasty generalization in theological arguments, such as using a single Quranic verse to derive a universal ruling without considering context.

Key Terms
مقدمات وعظية خطابية (muqaddimat wa'ziyya khitabiyya): Premises that are rhetorical and homiletic, used for persuasion rather than demonstration. [Note: Common classification; stable.]

C) Truth (حق)
D) Scientific certainty

Key Takeaway
The passage teaches that generalizations from a single or even many particulars yield only probability, not certitude, warning against hasty judgments in religious reasoning.

The Fallacy of Dialectical Premises in Theological Argumentation
Author identifies the second fallacy as using commonly accepted premises that are not certain.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify when a premise is accepted due to popularity, social pressure, or partisanship rather than certainty, and explain why it leads to error in theological reasoning.

Core Idea
Popular or commonly accepted premises are not automatically true; basing proofs on them leads to contradictions.

The author continues his analysis of fallacies by examining the second type: arguments that rely on premises accepted because people find their opposite repugnant. These 'famous' or 'dialectical' premises often masquerade as self-evident truths, but they lack certainty. This is especially dangerous in kalam, where theologians sometimes take verses literally without proper interpretation, leading to unsound syllogisms. Let's see how the author exposes this error.

Guided Path
1
Identify the type of premises described
The passage describes premises that are 'famous' (mashhura) and 'dialectical' (jadaliyya). They are accepted not because they are certain, but because their contradiction is considered ugly (istibsha').
These are not self-evident (awwaliyya) nor demonstrated (burhaniyya).
2
Recognize why they are accepted
Acceptance stems from two sources: violating the consensus of the masses (mukhalafat al-jamahir) or contradicting the apparent wording of the Quran. People find it shameful to reject such premises.
Social and scriptural pressure, not rational necessity, drives acceptance.
3
Analyze the mechanism of acceptance
The author notes that many people accept a premise simply because it is disgraceful to reject it, or because their mind recoils from its opposite. This is a psychological, not logical, basis.
Emotional or social aversion to the opposite does not make a premise true.
4
Apply the example: divine attributes
The example given is describing God with 'mercy' in the literal sense understood by common people. This leads to anthropomorphism (tashbih). The correct approach is ta'wil (interpretation) for such attributes.
Terms like 'mercy', 'anger', 'descending', 'coming' require interpretation in God's case.
5
See the consequence
If such a premise is taken literally and accepted without verification (tahqiq), a false conclusion follows. The premise is not self-evident nor proven by a valid syllogism.
The root error is abandoning ta'wil where it is required.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الثاني : فمؤلف من مقدمات مشهورة جدلية سلم بعضها من حيث استبشع نقيضها، إما لما فيه من مخالفة الجماهير وإما لما فيه من مخالفة ظاهر لفظ القرآن وكم من إنسان يسلم الشيء لأنه يستقبح منعه أو لأنه ينفر وهمه عن قبول نقيضه، وقد نبهنا على هذا في المقدمات وموضع المنع فيه وصف الله بالرحمة على الوجه الظاهر الذي فهمه العامة، والله تعالى مُقدّس عنه . بل لفظ الرحمة والغضب مُؤَوّل في حقه كلفظ النزول والمجيء وغيرهما، فإذا أخذ بالظاهر وسلم لا عن تحقيق لزمت النتيجة الكاذبة، وكونه رحيماً بالمعنى الذي تفهمه العامة مقدمة ليست أوّلية، وليس يدل عليها قياس بالشرط المذكور، فمحل الغلط ترك التأويل في محل وجوبه وعلى هذا ترى تناقض أكثر أقيسة المتكلمين، فإنهم مقدمات مُسلّمة لأجل الشهرة أو لتواضع المتعصبين لنصرة المذاهب ألفوها من غير برهان ومن غير كونها أولية واجبة التسليم.
As for the second: It is composed of famous, dialectical premises, some of which are accepted because their contradiction is considered ugly, either because it contradicts the masses or because it contradicts the apparent wording of the Quran. Many a person accepts something because it is disgraceful to reject it, or because his mind recoils from accepting its opposite. We have already pointed this out regarding premises, and the point of prevention is describing God with mercy in the apparent sense that the common people understand—God is exalted above that. Rather, the words 'mercy' and 'anger' are interpreted (ta'wil) in His case, like the words 'descending' and 'coming' and others. So if taken literally and accepted without verification, a false conclusion follows. And that He is merciful in the sense understood by the common people is not a self-evident premise, nor is it indicated by a syllogism with the mentioned condition. So the place of error is abandoning interpretation where it is required. And based on this, you see the contradiction in most syllogisms of the theologians, because they are composed of premises accepted for fame or due to the deference of partisans supporting doctrines, without demonstration and without being self-evident and necessarily acceptable.
Source pages p. 113

Commentary
This passage analyzes the second fallacy, which uses dialectical premises (مشهورة جدلية). These are premises accepted not because they are certain, but because people find their opposite repugnant—e.g., violating common belief or the apparent meaning of scripture. The author gives the example of describing God with 'mercy' in the literal sense understood by the common folk. The theologians (متكلمون) often commit this error: they take a verse like 'Allah is merciful' literally without ta'wil (interpretation) and then build syllogisms on it, leading to contradictions. The author insists that such premises are not self-evident (أولية) and are not proved by a valid syllogism. The root of error is neglecting proper hermeneutics when it is required. This warns against using popular but unexamined beliefs as foundations for logical arguments.

Key Terms
مقدمات مشهورة
تأويل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the source of error in the syllogisms of the theologians (mutakallimun)?
A) Using self-evident premises
B) Accepting premises due to fame or partisanship without demonstration
C) Using purely rhetorical premises
D) Misapplication of syllogistic form

Key Takeaway
A premise that is widely accepted or 'obvious' may still be false; sound argumentation requires premises that are certain, not merely famous.

Identifying Imaginary Premises (Muqaddima Wahmiyya)
Author identifies the fallacy of using imaginary premises.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify an imaginary (wahmi) premise in an argument and explain why it is unsuitable for demonstration.

Core Idea
Premises based on imagination lack the certainty required for demonstrative proofs.

In this lesson, we examine the fourth fallacy discussed by the author: using an imaginary premise. He gives the example 'Every existent is either connected to the world or separated' and declares it an imaginary premise that cannot serve as a demonstration. We will learn to spot such premises and understand why they are fallacious, especially in theological debates.

Guided Path
1
Identify the premise in question
The author presents the premise: 'Every existent is either connected to the world or separated.' This is the example of an imaginary premise.
2
Recognize it as imaginary (wahmi)
The author explicitly labels it 'muqaddima wahmiyya' – a premise arising from imagination, not rational certainty. Such premises are not self-evident nor derived from certain sources.
3
Understand why it fails
The premise presents a false dichotomy. Not all existents can be neatly categorized as connected or separated; there may be other possibilities or the distinction may be ill-defined. Hence it lacks necessity.
False dichotomies are common sources of imaginary premises.
4
See the broader impact
The author notes that the same type of imaginary premise underlies disputes like the indivisible atom (juz' la yatajaza'). This shows how such premises can mislead significant theological and philosophical arguments.
5
Apply the criterion
When you encounter an argument that relies on a dichotomy or a claim that seems intuitively plausible but is not rationally necessary, test whether it is an imaginary premise. If so, it cannot be used in a demonstrative proof.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الرابع وهو أن كلّ موجود فإما متصل بالعالم أو منفصل، فهي مقدمة وهمية ذكرنا وجه الغلط فيها وميّزنا الوهميات وبيَّنا أنها لا تصلح أن تجعل مقدمات في البراهين، وهو منشأ الضلال أيضاً في مسألة الجزء الذي لا يتجزأ ولكن ذكر الموضع الذي يغلط الوهم فيه طويل يستقصى في كتاب غير هذا الكتاب.
As for the fourth, which is that every existent is either connected to the world or separated—this is an imaginary (wahmi) premise. We have mentioned the place of error in it, distinguished imaginary premises, and shown that they are not suitable to be used as premises in demonstrations. And it is also the source of error in the question of the indivisible part, but detailing the place where the imagination errs is lengthy and requires a book other than this book.
Source pages p. 114

Commentary
This passage analyzes the fourth fallacy, which uses a premise that is imaginary (وهمية). The premise is the false dichotomy: 'Every existent is either connected to the world or separated.' This is not a certain premise; it arises from the imagination, not reason. The author says such premises are not suitable for demonstrations (براهين). He also notes that this same type of fallacy underlies the dispute about the indivisible atom (جزء لا يتجزأ). However, fully explaining how imagination misleads would require a separate book. This guards against accepting premises that are not rationally justified but only seem plausible due to mental imagery.

Key Terms

Imaginary premises, even when they appear exhaustive, cannot serve as foundations for demonstrative proofs because they lack rational necessity.

Resolving the Fallacy of Equivocation: The Definition of 'Lower'
Author resolves the paradox of the earth's suspension by clarifying the definition of 'lower'.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and resolve a fallacy caused by equivocation of the term 'asfal' (lower) by distinguishing between relative and absolute definitions.

Core Idea
Ambiguous definitions cause false conclusions; clarifying them reveals truth.

The author examines a common physical paradox: if everything heavy falls downward, why doesn't the earth fall? The error lies in the ambiguous meaning of 'lower'. This lesson will show you how to detect and correct such equivocation fallacies by examining the two possible definitions of 'asfal'.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Fallacious Argument
The argument states: 'Everything heavy inclines downward; the earth is heavy, so it should incline downward and pierce the air.' Recognize that the conclusion seems absurd, indicating a fallacy.
2
Locate the Ambiguous Term
The term 'asfal' (lower) is equivocal. The author presents two distinct definitions: one relative to human orientation (head/foot) and one absolute (center of the celestial sphere).
The ambiguity is the root of the fallacy.
3
Test Each Definition
Under the relative definition, the argument leads to absurdity (inverting a person swaps directions). Under the absolute definition, since the earth is already at the center (lowest point), it cannot move further down.
4
Determine the Correct Definition for Context
The author implies that in physics, the absolute definition (center of the sphere) is appropriate. This resolves the paradox: the earth is already at the lowest point, so it stands.
The fallacy arises from neglecting to specify which definition is used.
5
Apply to Other Arguments
In any argument, check if key terms have multiple meanings. If so, see if the conclusion changes under different definitions. The fallacy is resolved by clarifying the intended meaning.
This method applies to many debates in Islamic sciences.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الخامس : وهو وقوف الأرض في الهواء فلا استحالة فيه، وقول القائل : كلّ ثقيل فمائل إلى أسفل، والأرض ثقيلة فينبغي أن تميل إلى أسفل، ومن يلزم أن تخرق الهواء ولا تقف غلط مَنْشَؤُه إهمال لفظ الأسفل وأنه ما معناه، فإن الأسفل يقابله أعلى فلا بد من جهتين متقابلتين وتقابل الجهتين إما أن يكون بالإضافة إلى رأس الآدمي ورجله حتى لو لم يكن آدمي لم يكن أسفل ولا أعلى، ولو انتكس آدمي لصار جهة الأسفل أعلى وهو محال، وإما أن يكون الأسفل هو أبعد المواضع عن الفلك المحيط وهو المركز، والأعلى هو أقرب المواضع إلى المحيط، فإن صح هذا فالأرض إذا كانت في المركز فهي في أسفل سافلين، فلا أن تنتقل لأن أسفل سافلين غاية البعد عن المحيط وهو المركز، ومهما جاوزت المركز في أي جانب كان فارقت الأسفل إلى جهة الأعلى، فإن كان المعنى بالأسفل هذا فما ذكروه ليس بمحال وإن كان المعنى بالأعلى والأسفل ما يحاذي جهة رأسنا وقدمنا فما ذكروه محال، فتأمل جداً حدّ الأسفل حتى يتبيَّن لك أحد الأمرين، وإنما تعرف ذلك بالنظر في حقيقة الجهة وأنها تتحد أطرافها المتقابلة، ولا يمكن شرحه في هذا الكتاب. فإذن هذه الأغاليط نشأت من تسليم مقدمات ليست واجبة التسليم ومثاراتها قد جرى التنبيه عليها، فليقس بما ذكرناه ما لم نذكره.
As for the fifth, which is the earth's standing in air—there is no impossibility in it. And the statement of the one who says: 'Everything heavy inclines downward, and the earth is heavy, so it should incline downward, and it necessarily follows that it would pierce the air and not stand.' The error arises from neglecting the word 'lower' (asfal) and what it means. For 'lower' is opposite to 'higher,' so there must be two opposite directions. The opposition of the two directions is either relative to the human head and foot—such that if there were no human, there would be no 'lower' or 'higher,' and if a human were inverted, the direction of 'lower' would become 'higher,' which is absurd—or 'lower' is the farthest place from the surrounding sphere (the center), and 'higher' is the closest place to the sphere. If this is correct, then if the earth is at the center, it is in the lowest of the low (asfal safilin), so it cannot move, because the lowest of the low is the farthest from the sphere (the center), and whenever it passes the center on any side, it leaves the lower towards the higher. So if 'lower' means this, what they mentioned is not impossible. But if 'higher' and 'lower' mean what is aligned with the direction of our head and foot, then what they mentioned is impossible. So reflect deeply on the definition of 'lower' so that one of the two becomes clear to you. You can only know that by examining the reality of direction and that its opposite ends are united. It cannot be explained in this book. So these fallacies arise from accepting premises that are not necessarily acceptable. Their sources have been warned about. So by what we have mentioned, let what we have not mentioned be measured.
Source pages p. 114

Commentary
This passage resolves the fifth fallacy concerning the earth's suspension in air. The argument is: heavy things move downwards; the earth is heavy, so it should move downwards and not stand. The author says the error is due to neglecting the meaning of 'asfal' (lower). He provides two possible definitions: one relative to human orientation (head vs. foot), which is subjective and would make the argument implausible; the other absolute: the lowest point is the center of the celestial sphere. If 'lower' means the center, then the earth being at the center means it is already at the lowest point, so it cannot move further down. Thus, the original argument fails due to equivocation. This demonstrates that many logical paradoxes dissolve when terms are properly defined. The author concludes by stating that all these fallacies arise from accepting premises that are not necessarily acceptable, and that the reader should apply the same method to other cases.

Key Terms
أسفل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What does the author identify as the source of error in the argument that the earth must fall?
A) The premise that heavy things incline downward is false
B) Neglecting the meaning of 'lower' (asfal) and its definitions
C) The syllogism has an invalid form
D) The earth is not heavy

Key Takeaway
Many fallacies arise from ambiguous terms; defining them clearly resolves the paradox.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Apply the same method to a fiqh argument: 'All intoxicants are forbidden; nabidh is intoxicating, so nabidh is forbidden.' Consider that 'intoxicating' might have a narrow (khamr) or broad (any substance) definition. How does the conclusion change?
?
In a kalam debate, an opponent argues: 'The world is composed of atoms, but atoms cannot be created ex nihilo, so the world is eternal.' A mutakalim responds by defining 'atom' differently (e.g., as the indivisible part that is created). Identify the equivocation and show how the corrected definition affects the argument.

Fallacy of Certainty Not Voluntary
Ghazali refutes the claim that certainty is not voluntary by exposing a false premise.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the logical error in the argument that certainty cannot be voluntary because it occurs in sleep, and explain why a valid syllogistic form does not guarantee true premises.

Core Idea
A valid syllogism can be unsound if its premises are false.

Ghazali addresses a common objection against the voluntary nature of certainty. The mutakallimun argue that since certainty occurs in sleep, it cannot be voluntary. Ghazali shows that while the syllogistic form is correct, the premise that voluntary acts never occur in sleep is false. He also refutes a stronger claim about the inability to refrain from voluntary acts at all times.

Guided Path
1
Identify the argument structure
The argument is a conditional syllogism: If certainty were voluntary, it would not occur in sleep; it does occur in sleep; therefore it is not voluntary. Recognize that this is modus tollens.
2
Check syllogistic validity
The form of modus tollens is valid: denying the consequent allows denying the antecedent. Therefore, the logical structure is correct.
3
Examine the major premise
The premise 'If voluntary, then not in sleep' is claimed to be false. Ghazali provides counterexamples: people walk and speak in their sleep voluntarily. Thus, the premise is not universally true.
Consider whether the premise holds for all voluntary acts.
4
Analyze the second objection
Another objection: if voluntary, one could always refrain. Ghazali counters with examples like eating and urination, which are voluntary but not always avoidable. So the implication fails.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الثالث : فاليقين والصحيح أنه فعل إرادي، وقول من قال : لو كان إرادياً لما كان يحصل في النوم ولكنه يحصل فيه فليس بإرادي، فهو شرطي متصل استثني فيه نقيض التالي واستنتج نقيض المقدم، فصورة القياس صحيحة ولكن لزوم التالي للمقدم غير مسلم، فإن الفعل الإرادي قد يحصل في النوم فكم من نائم يمشي خطوات مرتبة ويتكلم بكلمات منظومة . وقوله : لو كان إرادياً لقدر على الامتناع منه في كل وقت فغير مسلم بل يأكل الإنسان ويبول بالإرادة ولا يقدر على الامتناع في كلّ ،وقت فإن قُيّد بكل وقت كان كاذباً ولم يسلم لزوم التالي للمقدم. وأما الرابع وهو أن كلّ موجود فإما متصل بالعالم أو منفصل، فهي مقدمة وهمية ذكرنا وجه الغلط فيها وميّزنا الوهميات وبيَّنا أنها لا تصلح أن تجعل مقدمات في البراهين، وهو منشأ الضلال أيضاً في مسألة الجزء الذي لا يتجزأ ولكن ذكر الموضع الذي يغلط الوهم فيه طويل يستقصى في كتاب غير هذا الكتاب. وأما الخامس : وهو وقوف الأرض في الهواء فلا استحالة فيه، وقول القائل : كلّ ثقيل فمائل إلى أسفل، والأرض ثقيلة فينبغي أن تميل إلى أسفل، ومن يلزم أن تخرق الهواء ولا تقف غلط مَنْشَؤُه إهمال لفظ الأسفل وأنه ما معناه، فإن الأسفل يقابله أعلى فلا بد من جهتين متقابلتين وتقابل الجهتين إما أن يكون بالإضافة إلى رأس الآدمي ورجله حتى لو لم يكن آدمي لم يكن أسفل ولا أعلى، ولو انتكس آدمي لصار جهة الأسفل أعلى وهو محال، وإما أن يكون الأسفل هو أبعد المواضع عن الفلك المحيط وهو المركز، والأعلى هو أقرب المواضع إلى المحيط، فإن صح هذا فالأرض إذا كانت في المركز فهي في أسفل سافلين، فلا أن تنتقل لأن أسفل سافلين غاية البعد عن المحيط وهو المركز، ومهما جاوزت المركز في أي جانب كان فارقت الأسفل إلى جهة الأعلى، فإن كان المعنى بالأسفل هذا فما ذكروه ليس بمحال وإن كان المعنى بالأعلى والأسفل ما يحاذي جهة رأسنا وقدمنا فما ذكروه محال، فتأمل جداً حدّ الأسفل حتى يتبيَّن لك أحد الأمرين، وإنما تعرف ذلك بالنظر في حقيقة الجهة وأنها تتحد أطرافها المتقابلة، ولا يمكن شرحه في هذا الكتاب. فإذن هذه الأغاليط نشأت من تسليم مقدمات ليست واجبة التسليم ومثاراتها قد جرى التنبيه عليها، فليقس بما ذكرناه ما لم نذكره .
As for the third: Certainty and the correct [view] is that it is a voluntary act. The statement of one who said: "If it were voluntary, it would not occur in sleep, but it does occur therein, so it is not voluntary" is a conditional conjunctive [syllogism] in which the contradiction of the consequent is posited and the contradiction of the antecedent is derived. The form of the syllogism is correct, but the implication of the consequent from the antecedent is not conceded, for a voluntary act may occur in sleep – how many a sleeper takes ordered steps and speaks ordered words! And his statement: "If it were voluntary, he would be able to refrain from it at every time" is not conceded. Rather, a person eats and urinates voluntarily but is not able to refrain at every time. If it is restricted to every time, it is false, and the implication of the consequent from the antecedent is not conceded. As for the fourth: that every existent is either connected to the realm or separate, this is an imaginal premise (muqaddima wahmiyya). We have mentioned the aspect of error in it and distinguished the imaginal premises and shown that they are not suitable to be used as premises in demonstrations. It is also the source of error in the problem of the indivisible part (al-juz' alladhi la yatajaza'), but mentioning the place where the imagination errs is lengthy and will be investigated in a book other than this one. As for the fifth: the suspension of the earth in the air – there is no impossibility in it. The statement of the speaker: "Every heavy thing is inclined downward, and the earth is heavy, so it should incline downward, and consequently it should pierce the air and not stand" – the error originates from neglecting the term "downward" and what it means. For "downward" is opposed by "upward", so there must be two opposite directions. The opposition of the two directions is either relative to the head and foot of a human, such that if there were no human, there would be no down or up, and if a human were inverted, the direction of down would become up, which is impossible; or "downward" is the farthest place from the encompassing sphere, i.e., the center, and "upward" is the nearest place to the sphere. If this is correct, then the earth, being at the center, is in the lowest of the low, so it should not move, because the lowest of the low is the utmost distance from the sphere, which is the center. Whenever it passes the center on any side, it leaves the down to the direction of up. If the meaning of "downward" is this, then what they mentioned is not impossible. If the meaning of "upward" and "downward" is what is aligned with the direction of our head and feet, then what they mentioned is impossible. So reflect deeply on the definition of "downward" so that one of the two matters becomes clear to you. You only know that by considering the reality of direction and that its opposite extremities are one, and it cannot be explained in this book. So these fallacies arose from conceding premises that are not necessary to concede, and their sources have been pointed out. So analogize what we have not mentioned to what we have mentioned.
Source pages p. 114

Commentary
This passage classifies and refutes three specific fallacies from the mutakallimun. The author first addresses the fallacy that certainty is not voluntary because it occurs in sleep, showing that the conditional syllogism's form is correct but the premise that voluntary acts do not occur in sleep is false. He provides counterexamples like walking and speaking in sleep. He also rejects the additional claim that one can always refrain from voluntary acts, using eating and urination as examples. The fourth fallacy is the premise that every existent is either connected or disconnected from the world; this is identified as an imaginal premise (wahmiyya) that is unsuitable for demonstration and is the source of error in the atomism debate. The fifth fallacy concerns the earth's suspension; the error is due to ambiguity in the term 'downward'. The author distinguishes two meanings: relative to human orientation and astronomical (center of sphere). If the latter, the earth's position is natural and no movement is required. The passage concludes that these fallacies stem from conceding unjustified premises, and the reader should apply the same analysis elsewhere.

Key Terms
اليقين (al-yaqin): Certainty; Ghazali argues it is a voluntary act (fi'l iradi) and refutes objections based on sleep. [Note: In earlier logic, yaqin often means certain knowledge; Ghazali particularizes it as voluntary here.]
فعل إرادي (fi'l iradi): Voluntary act; used to argue that certainty is acquired through effort, not automatically. [Note: Stable across Islamic philosophy.]
شرطي متصل (sharti muttasil): Conditional conjunctive syllogism; Ghazali analyzes the form of the argument about certainty in sleep. [Note: Standard term for hypothetical syllogism.]

B) The premise that voluntary acts do not occur in sleep is false.
C) Certainty does not actually occur in sleep.
D) The argument fails to prove that certainty is voluntary.

Key Takeaway
A valid syllogistic form does not guarantee a true conclusion; one must also verify the truth of the premises.

Fallacy of Imaginal Premises
Ghazali warns against using imaginal premises in demonstrative arguments.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify an imaginal premise (muqaddima wahmiyya) and explain why it is unsuitable for demonstration.

Core Idea
Imaginal premises, arising from the estimative faculty, undermine demonstrative reasoning.

Ghazali identifies a second fallacy: the claim that every existent is either connected or separate from the world. He calls this an imaginal premise—one that comes from the wahm (estimative faculty) and not from certain knowledge. Such premises, he warns, cannot be used in burhani proofs and are the source of errors in other debates like atomism.

Guided Path
1
Identify the premise
The premise is: 'Every existent is either connected to the world or separate.' This is presented as a universal disjunction.
2
Understand the source as wahmi
Ghazali labels this an 'imaginal premise' (muqaddima wahmiyya). It springs from the wahm, not from rational necessity or empirical certainty.
The wahm often produces false dichotomies.
3
Recognize the consequence for demonstration
Such premises are unsuitable for demonstrations (burhan). They lead to errors, like the debate on the indivisible part. Knowing this helps avoid accepting them as certain.
Ghazali refers to another work for a detailed discussion on the indivisible part.

Fallacy of Earth's Suspension: Ambiguity of 'Downward'
Ghazali refutes the earth-suspension argument by exposing ambiguity in 'downward'.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to detect fallacies arising from ambiguous terms and resolve them by distinguishing different meanings.

Core Idea
The term 'downward' is ambiguous; clarifying its meaning dissolves the apparent contradiction in the earth's suspension.

The fifth fallacy concerns the earth's suspension in air. Mutakallimun argued that since heavy things fall, the earth should fall through the air. Ghazali shows that the error lies in the ambiguity of 'downward' (asfal). He distinguishes between relative down (head-feet orientation) and absolute down (center of the universe). The earth, being at the center, is already at the lowest point, so no motion is required. This lesson teaches how semantic clarity resolves fallacies.

Guided Path
1
Identify the argument
The argument: Every heavy thing inclines downward; the earth is heavy; therefore it should move downward and fall through the air.
2
Locate the ambiguous term
The term 'downward' (asfal) is ambiguous. It could mean relative to human orientation or absolute center of the universe.
3
Distinguish the meanings
Relative meaning: 'down' is towards the feet; absolute meaning: 'down' is the center of the celestial sphere. Ghazali explains both.
4
Evaluate based on each meaning
Under the absolute meaning, the earth is already at the center (lowest point), so it should not move. Under the relative meaning, the conclusion would follow, but that meaning is not applicable to the universe. Thus, the argument's conclusion depends on an unclarified term.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الثالث : فاليقين والصحيح أنه فعل إرادي، وقول من قال : لو كان إرادياً لما كان يحصل في النوم ولكنه يحصل فيه فليس بإرادي، فهو شرطي متصل استثني فيه نقيض التالي واستنتج نقيض المقدم، فصورة القياس صحيحة ولكن لزوم التالي للمقدم غير مسلم، فإن الفعل الإرادي قد يحصل في النوم فكم من نائم يمشي خطوات مرتبة ويتكلم بكلمات منظومة . وقوله : لو كان إرادياً لقدر على الامتناع منه في كل وقت فغير مسلم بل يأكل الإنسان ويبول بالإرادة ولا يقدر على الامتناع في كلّ ،وقت فإن قُيّد بكل وقت كان كاذباً ولم يسلم لزوم التالي للمقدم. وأما الرابع وهو أن كلّ موجود فإما متصل بالعالم أو منفصل، فهي مقدمة وهمية ذكرنا وجه الغلط فيها وميّزنا الوهميات وبيَّنا أنها لا تصلح أن تجعل مقدمات في البراهين، وهو منشأ الضلال أيضاً في مسألة الجزء الذي لا يتجزأ ولكن ذكر الموضع الذي يغلط الوهم فيه طويل يستقصى في كتاب غير هذا الكتاب. وأما الخامس : وهو وقوف الأرض في الهواء فلا استحالة فيه، وقول القائل : كلّ ثقيل فمائل إلى أسفل، والأرض ثقيلة فينبغي أن تميل إلى أسفل، ومن يلزم أن تخرق الهواء ولا تقف غلط مَنْشَؤُه إهمال لفظ الأسفل وأنه ما معناه، فإن الأسفل يقابله أعلى فلا بد من جهتين متقابلتين وتقابل الجهتين إما أن يكون بالإضافة إلى رأس الآدمي ورجله حتى لو لم يكن آدمي لم يكن أسفل ولا أعلى، ولو انتكس آدمي لصار جهة الأسفل أعلى وهو محال، وإما أن يكون الأسفل هو أبعد المواضع عن الفلك المحيط وهو المركز، والأعلى هو أقرب المواضع إلى المحيط، فإن صح هذا فالأرض إذا كانت في المركز فهي في أسفل سافلين، فلا أن تنتقل لأن أسفل سافلين غاية البعد عن المحيط وهو المركز، ومهما جاوزت المركز في أي جانب كان فارقت الأسفل إلى جهة الأعلى، فإن كان المعنى بالأسفل هذا فما ذكروه ليس بمحال وإن كان المعنى بالأعلى والأسفل ما يحاذي جهة رأسنا وقدمنا فما ذكروه محال، فتأمل جداً حدّ الأسفل حتى يتبيَّن لك أحد الأمرين، وإنما تعرف ذلك بالنظر في حقيقة الجهة وأنها تتحد أطرافها المتقابلة، ولا يمكن شرحه في هذا الكتاب. فإذن هذه الأغاليط نشأت من تسليم مقدمات ليست واجبة التسليم ومثاراتها قد جرى التنبيه عليها، فليقس بما ذكرناه ما لم نذكره .
As for the third: Certainty and the correct [view] is that it is a voluntary act. The statement of one who said: "If it were voluntary, it would not occur in sleep, but it does occur therein, so it is not voluntary" is a conditional conjunctive [syllogism] in which the contradiction of the consequent is posited and the contradiction of the antecedent is derived. The form of the syllogism is correct, but the implication of the consequent from the antecedent is not conceded, for a voluntary act may occur in sleep – how many a sleeper takes ordered steps and speaks ordered words! And his statement: "If it were voluntary, he would be able to refrain from it at every time" is not conceded. Rather, a person eats and urinates voluntarily but is not able to refrain at every time. If it is restricted to every time, it is false, and the implication of the consequent from the antecedent is not conceded. As for the fourth: that every existent is either connected to the realm or separate, this is an imaginal premise (muqaddima wahmiyya). We have mentioned the aspect of error in it and distinguished the imaginal premises and shown that they are not suitable to be used as premises in demonstrations. It is also the source of error in the problem of the indivisible part (al-juz' alladhi la yatajaza'), but mentioning the place where the imagination errs is lengthy and will be investigated in a book other than this one. As for the fifth: the suspension of the earth in the air – there is no impossibility in it. The statement of the speaker: "Every heavy thing is inclined downward, and the earth is heavy, so it should incline downward, and consequently it should pierce the air and not stand" – the error originates from neglecting the term "downward" and what it means. For "downward" is opposed by "upward", so there must be two opposite directions. The opposition of the two directions is either relative to the head and foot of a human, such that if there were no human, there would be no down or up, and if a human were inverted, the direction of down would become up, which is impossible; or "downward" is the farthest place from the encompassing sphere, i.e., the center, and "upward" is the nearest place to the sphere. If this is correct, then the earth, being at the center, is in the lowest of the low, so it should not move, because the lowest of the low is the utmost distance from the sphere, which is the center. Whenever it passes the center on any side, it leaves the down to the direction of up. If the meaning of "downward" is this, then what they mentioned is not impossible. If the meaning of "upward" and "downward" is what is aligned with the direction of our head and feet, then what they mentioned is impossible. So reflect deeply on the definition of "downward" so that one of the two matters becomes clear to you. You only know that by considering the reality of direction and that its opposite extremities are one, and it cannot be explained in this book. So these fallacies arose from conceding premises that are not necessary to concede, and their sources have been pointed out. So analogize what we have not mentioned to what we have mentioned.
Source pages p. 114

Commentary
This passage classifies and refutes three specific fallacies from the mutakallimun. The author first addresses the fallacy that certainty is not voluntary because it occurs in sleep, showing that the conditional syllogism's form is correct but the premise that voluntary acts do not occur in sleep is false. He provides counterexamples like walking and speaking in sleep. He also rejects the additional claim that one can always refrain from voluntary acts, using eating and urination as examples. The fourth fallacy is the premise that every existent is either connected or disconnected from the world; this is identified as an imaginal premise (wahmiyya) that is unsuitable for demonstration and is the source of error in the atomism debate. The fifth fallacy concerns the earth's suspension; the error is due to ambiguity in the term 'downward'. The author distinguishes two meanings: relative to human orientation and astronomical (center of sphere). If the latter, the earth's position is natural and no movement is required. The passage concludes that these fallacies stem from conceding unjustified premises, and the reader should apply the same analysis elsewhere.

Key Terms
أسفل (asfal): Downward; Ghazali shows its ambiguity between relative direction (head/feet) and absolute center of the universe. [Note: The physical concept of 'down' varies across cosmological models; Ghazali's analysis is context-specific.]

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Ghazali, what is the error in the argument that the earth must fall because it is heavy?
A) The earth is not heavy.
B) The term 'downward' is ambiguous.
C) The syllogism form is invalid.
D) The earth is not in the air.

Key Takeaway
Many fallacies arise from ambiguous terms; clarifying definitions is essential for sound reasoning.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Find an ambiguous term in a theological argument (e.g., 'istiwa' in Quran 20:5) and explain how different interpretations lead to different conclusions. Use Ghazali's method of distinguishing meanings.
?
Practise: Rephrase the earth-suspension argument with a clear definition of 'downward' and test whether the conclusion holds.

Sophistry and Fallacies
