The Fallacy of Ignorance Regarding a Particular Instance
Author refutes the fallacy of ignorance regarding a particular instance.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and refute the fallacy of confusing ignorance of a particular instance with the falsity of a universal statement.

Core Idea
A universal truth remains true even if you don't know the status of a particular instance.

In this passage, the author tackles a common sophistical move: someone objects to a universal claim by pointing out that the particular case is unknown. For example, if you claim 'every two is even', the objector says, 'But you don't know if the thing in my hand is two, so you can't say it's even.' The author shows why this objection is invalid and clarifies the proper logical response.

Guided Path
1
Identify the universal claim
Find the universal statement in the passage: 'Every two is even' (كل اثنين زوج). This is a universal affirmative proposition that the author holds as true.
2
Identify the objection
The objection is: 'We do not know that what is in your hand is two, so you cannot conclude it is even.' This is the fallacy: ignorance of a particular instance is used to challenge the universal.
3
Recognize the error
The author declares this objection invalid. The universal 'every two is even' is true independently of our knowledge of any specific two. Not knowing a particular does not make the universal false.
4
Formulate the correct response
The correct response is: 'If what is in your hand is two, then it is even.' If asked whether it is two, we may say 'I don't know.' This ignorance does not contradict the universal. The universal is only contradicted by asserting 'some two is not even' or 'no two is even.'
5
Apply the lesson to Islamic sciences
In Usul al-Fiqh, a similar fallacy might arise: 'Every intoxicant is haram. We do not know if this drink is intoxicating, so we cannot say it is haram.' The proper response: the universal rule remains; we must investigate whether the specific drink is intoxicating.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
النظر الثالث : في المغلظات في القياس
نعرفه اثنين فهو زوج، وما في يدك لم نعرف أنه اثنان وهذا الجواب فاسد بل كلّ اثنين فهو في نفسه زوج، سواء عرفناه أو لم نعرفه لكن الجواب أن نقول : إن كان ما في يدك اثنين فهو زوج. فإن قلت : فهل هو اثنان فأقول : لا أدري. وهذا الجهل لا يُضاد قولي إن كل اثنين زوج بل ضدّه أن أقول : كل اثنين ليس بزوج أو بعض الاثنين ليس بزوج. فإذن ينبغي أن نتعرف أنه هل هو اثنان، فإن عرفنا أنه اثنان علمنا أنه زوج وأخطرنا ذلك بالبال
The third consideration: On fallacies in syllogism. We know it to be two, so it is even, and what is in your hand we do not know that it is two, and this answer is invalid. Rather, every two is in itself even, whether we know it or not. But the answer is that we say: If what is in your hand is two, then it is even. If you say: is it two? I say: I do not know. And this ignorance does not contradict my saying that every two is even, rather its opposite is that I say: every two is not even, or some two is not even. Therefore, we must ascertain whether it is two; if we know it to be two, we know it is even and bring that to mind.
Source pages p. 119

Commentary
This passage is from the third consideration on fallacies in syllogism. The author presents and refutes a common fallacy: the objection that if someone does not know whether something is two, they cannot know it is even. The author first states the objection: 'If we know it to be two, then it is even, but what is in your hand we do not know is two.' The author declares this response invalid. Rather, every two is in itself even, whether we know it or not. The correct answer is: if what is in your hand is two, then it is even. If someone asks: 'Is it two?', the response is: 'I don't know.' This ignorance does not contradict my statement that every two is even. Contradiction would be if I said 'no two is even' or 'some two is not even.' Therefore, we should investigate whether it is two. If we know it is two, we know it is even and bring that to mind. The key point: ignorance of a particular fact does not invalidate a universal statement. This guards against the fallacy of confusing a universal truth with our knowledge of its instantiation.

Key Terms
مغلطات (mughellatāt): Fallacies or deceptive arguments in syllogism. [Note: Stable term for fallacies across the logical tradition.]
زوج (zawj): Even number, as opposed to odd (fard). [Note: No significant shift.]

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why is the objection 'we do not know if it is two' not a valid refutation of 'every two is even'?
A) Because the universal 'every two is even' is known by definition, not by investigation.
B) Because the truth of the universal is independent of our knowledge of particular instances.
C) Because the objector must prove that the thing is not two, not merely that it is unknown.
D) Because the universal is only probable, not certain.

Key Takeaway
Not knowing whether a particular instance falls under a universal does not falsify the universal; it only calls for investigation of that instance.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

The Self-Refutation of Taqlid Advocates
Author refutes those who reject rational inquiry and demand taqlid.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the self-refutation argument against those who reject rational inquiry and reconstruct it as a conditional syllogism.

Core Idea
Rejecting reason requires reason – the advocate of taqlid contradicts himself in the very act of arguing.

This passage from the Mi'yar al-'Ilm refutes those who claim that rational inquiry (nazar) should be abandoned in favor of blind following (taqlid). The author shows that such a position is self-defeating: the opponent must use reason to argue against reason, and moreover, any claim to follow a particular prophet can only be justified by rational reflection. We will unpack this argument and see how it exposes the inconsistency.

Guided Path
1
Identify the opponent's claim
The taqlid advocate says rational reflection leads to disagreement and uncertainty, so it is safer to follow authority blindly. Locate this claim in the Arabic passage.
The opponent argues that because intellects differ, taqlid is the safe path.
2
Recognize the first inconsistency
The opponent debates their position, which requires using reason. Thus, they contradict themselves by using reason to deny reason. Find the author's point about the sophist's debating.
The author says: 'How can the sophist debate; his debating itself is an acknowledgment of the path of reflection.'
3
Reconstruct the conditional argument
The author poses a dilemma: either your taqlid of your prophet is like that of Jews and Christians (allowing possible falsehood), or it is different (requiring rational justification). If it is like theirs, you admit you might be wrong (kufr). If it is different, you must have a rational basis. Either way, you rely on reason.
Formalize as: If your taqlid is like theirs, then you allow falsehood. You do not allow falsehood. Therefore, your taqlid is not like theirs, so you must have a rational basis.
4
Formulate the conclusion
The opponent cannot consistently reject rational inquiry because their own position requires it to justify their prophet's truth. The author concludes they are worse than sophists, affirming and denying reason simultaneously.
The passage states they 'affirm by denying reflection and deny when they affirm reflection.'

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فالسوفسطائي كيف يناظر ومناظرته في نفسه اعتراف بطريق النظر، ولا ينبغي يتعجب من اعتقاد السفسطة والحيرة مع وضوح المعقولات، فإن ذلك لا يتفق إلَّا على الندور لمصاب في عقله بآفة؛ فإنا نشاهد جماعة من أرباب المذاهب هم السوفسطائية والناس غافلون عنهم، فكلّ من يناظر في إيجاب التقليد أو إبطال النظر سوفسطائي في الزجر عن النظر لا مستند لهم، إلا أن العقول لا ثقة بها والاختلاف فيها كثير، فسلوك طريق الأمن وهو التقليد أولى . فإذا قيل لهم : فهل قلّدتم صدق نبيكم وتميزون بينه وبين الكاذب أم تقليدكم كتقليد اليهود والنصارى، فإن كان كتقليدهم فقد جوّزتم كونكم مبطلين وهذا كفر عندكم، وإن لم تجوّزوه فتعرفونه بالضرورة أو بنظر العقل، فإن عرفتموه بالنظر فقد أثبتم النظر. وقد اختلف الناس في هذا النظر وهو تصديق الأنبياء كما اختلفوا في سائر النظريات . وفي إثبات صدق الأنبياء بالمعجزات من الأغوار والأغماض ما لا يكاد يخفى على النظار، وبهذا الاعتقاد صاروا أخس رتبة من السوفسطائي فإنهم مثبتون بإنكار النظر ونافون إذا أثبتوا النظر في معرفة صدق النبي .
How can the sophist debate; his debating itself is an acknowledgment of the path of reflection. And one should not be amazed at the belief in sophistry and confusion despite the clarity of intelligibles, for that occurs only rarely due to a person afflicted with a defect in his intellect. For we see a group from the followers of doctrines who are the sophists, and people are heedless of them. So whoever debates about obligating taqlid or invalidating reflection is a sophist in deterring from reflection, with no support for them, except that intellects are not reliable and disagreement in them is great, so taking the path of safety—which is taqlid—is preferable. Then if it is said to them: Have you followed blindly the truth of your prophet and distinguished between him and the liar, or is your taqlid like the taqlid of the Jews and Christians? If it is like their taqlid, then you have allowed the possibility that you are false, and that is disbelief according to you. And if you do not allow that, then you know him by necessity or by rational reflection. If you know him by reflection, then you have affirmed reflection. And people have disagreed about this reflection, which is the assent to prophets, just as they disagreed about all theoretical matters. And in proving the truth of prophets through miracles, there are profundities and obscurities that are hardly hidden from thinkers. And by this belief, they have become lower in rank than the sophist, for they affirm by denying reflection and deny when they affirm reflection in knowing the truth of a prophet.
Source pages p. 120

Commentary
This passage is a critical refutation of those who reject rational inquiry (nazar) and advocate blind following (taqlid). The author begins by pointing out that the sophist's own act of debating presupposes the use of reason, so they contradict themselves. He notes that such sophistry only occurs in rare cases of intellectual defect. He then addresses the specific group that argues for taqlid on the grounds that reason leads to disagreement and is unreliable. The author uses a dialectical argument: if they follow their prophet blindly like Jews and Christians follow theirs, then they must admit the possibility that they are wrong, which is tantamount to disbelief. If they claim a special certainty, they must know it either by necessity or by reason. If by reason, then they have established the validity of rational inquiry. The author also mentions that proving prophethood through miracles involves deep considerations, and those who deny reason while using it to affirm their prophet are worse than sophists, as they are incoherent.

Key Terms
السوفسطائي (al-sūfisṭā'ī): Sophist; one who engages in sophistry, often denying the possibility of certain knowledge. [Note: In the Islamic tradition, al-sūfisṭā'ī refers to ancient Greek sophists and those who deny the reliability of reason.]
التقليد (al-taqlīd): Blind following of authority without evidence or reasoning. [Note: In fiqh, taqlid has a more nuanced meaning, but here it represents uncritical acceptance.]
النظر (al-naẓar): Rational reflection or intellectual inquiry aimed at acquiring knowledge. [Note: In kalam, nazar often refers to speculative reasoning about theological matters.]

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the author, why is the position of those who reject rational inquiry and demand taqlid inconsistent?
A) They use reason to argue against reason.
B) They cannot distinguish between true and false prophets without reason.
C) Their taqlid of their prophet is indistinguishable from the taqlid of Jews and Christians, which they consider false.
D) All of the above.

Key Takeaway
The advocate of taqlid cannot consistently reject rational inquiry because any claim to follow a particular prophet requires rational justification, and the very act of arguing against reason uses reason.

?
2. Consider a modern objection: 'Religious knowledge is purely by faith, not reason.' How would you apply the self-refutation argument from this lesson to expose the inconsistency?

Exposing the Batiniyya's Hypocrisy in Denying Reason
Author criticizes the Batiniyya for denying reason while using it to establish their imam's infallibility.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the Batiniyya's inconsistency in rejecting rational inquiry while using it to defend their imam's infallibility, and recognize this as a form of sophistry.

Core Idea
The Batiniyya reject reason while secretly relying on it—a self-refuting position.

In this passage, the author exposes a logical flaw in the Batiniyya sect. They claim rational inquiry is invalid due to widespread disagreement among thinkers, yet when asked to prove their imam's infallibility, they resort to the very reasoning they reject. This lesson helps you spot such hypocrisy and understand why it undermines their entire position.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Batiniyya's main claim
They assert that rational inquiry is invalid because of the multitude of disagreements among thinkers. This is their basis for rejecting reason.
2
Note their exception for the imam
Despite rejecting reason, they advocate blind following of an infallible imam (al-ma‘sūm). They hold that this imam is infallible and must be followed.
3
Uncover the inconsistency
When asked how they know the imam is infallible, they use types of reasoning (al-ẓanniyyāt) that are themselves subject to disagreement—the very thing they condemned.
They do not apply their own criterion of disagreement to their proof of infallibility.
4
Recognize the self-refutation
If all rational inquiry is invalid due to disagreement, then their own proof for infallibility is also invalid. Their position is self-refuting: they rely on reason to argue against reason.
5
Compare to sophistry
The author likens this to sophistry (sūfisṭāʾiyya), where one denies universal knowledge while making knowledge claims. The Batiniyya's inconsistency runs similar to madness.
The author dismisses them as unworthy of debate due to the baseness of their reasoning.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما السوفسطائي فقد طرد قياسه في إنكار المعرفة الكلية، ومن هذا الجنس باطنية الزمان فإنهم خدعوا بكثرة الاختلافات بين النظار، ودعوا إلى اعتقاد بطلان نظر العقل ثم دعوا إلى تقليد إمامهم المعصوم . وإذا قيل لهم : بماذا عرفتم عصمة إمامكم وليس يمكن دعوى الضرورة فيه؟ دعوا فيه إلى أنواع من النظر يشترك استعمالها في الظنيات، ولا تعرض على الاثنين إلا ويختلفان فيها، ولا يستدلون بكونه نظرياً واقعاً في محلّ الاختلاف على بطلانه، ويحكمون على سائر النظريات بالبطلان لتطرّق الخلاف فيها، وهذا وأمثاله العقل فيجري مجرى الجنون ولكن لا يُسمّى جنوناً والجنون فنون، والذين ينخدعون بأمثال هذه الخيالات هم أخس من أن نشتغل بمناظرتهم؛ فلنقتصر على ما ذكرناه في بيان أسباب الحيرة، والله أعلم .
As for the sophist, he has taken his syllogism to the extreme in denying universal knowledge. And of this kind are the Batiniyya of the time, for they were deceived by the multitude of disagreements among thinkers, and they called for believing in the invalidity of rational inquiry, then they called for following their infallible imam. And if it is said to them: By what did you know the infallibility of your imam, and it is not possible to claim necessity in it? They call for types of inquiry the use of which are shared in probable matters, and it is not presented to two people except that they differ in it. And they do not infer from it being theoretical and subject to disagreement its invalidity, and they judge all other theoretical matters to be invalid due to the occurrence of disagreement in them. And this and the like to the intellect runs the course of madness but is not called madness, and madness is of various kinds. And those who are deceived by such fantasies are too base for us to occupy ourselves with debating them. So let us suffice with what we have mentioned in explaining the causes of confusion. And God knows best.
Source pages p. 120

Commentary
This passage critiques the Batiniyya, a group that denies rational inquiry because of widespread disagreement among thinkers, yet they advocate blind following of an infallible imam. The author points out their inconsistency: when asked how they know their imam is infallible, they resort to types of reasoning that are themselves subject to disagreement—the very thing they reject. They hypocritically dismiss all theoretical matters as invalid because of disagreement, but they do not apply the same standard to their own reasoning about the imam. The author compares this behavior to madness and dismisses them as unworthy of debate. This passage guards against the fallacy of using disagreement to reject reason altogether while inconsistently relying on it for one's own beliefs.

Key Terms
الباطنية

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the Batiniyya's inconsistency?
A) They reject reason but accept revelation.
B) They claim their imam is infallible without proof.
C) They criticize rational inquiry for being subject to disagreement, yet use it to prove the imam's infallibility.
D) They follow their own reasoning but forbid others from doing so.

Key Takeaway
Denying reason while implicitly relying on it is a self-refuting position, and such sophistry must be exposed.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Task 1: Read the following claim: 'Reason is unreliable because people disagree on many matters.' Then the same person says: 'I know through reason that the imam is infallible.' Identify the inconsistency and explain why this is self-refuting.
?
Task 2: Find an example in contemporary discourse where someone dismisses a field (e.g., hadith criticism) due to differences of opinion, but then relies on that field to support their own view. How would you use the lesson's framework to critique them?

Sophistry and Fallacies
intermediate
8 min
Does Fire Really Burn? The Dialectics of Active Potency
Author addresses occasionalist objection to active potency.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct the occasionalist objection against active potency and explain the author's response regarding the semantic nature of definitions.

Core Idea
The author defines active potency as a term, not a reality—a key move to avoid confusion between philosophy and theology.

This passage exemplifies dialectical reasoning in Islamic theology. An occasionalist objects to the concept of active potency (like fire's power to burn) because, in his worldview, fire does not cause burning; God creates it directly. The author responds by clarifying that his earlier discussion was merely about the meaning of the term, not its real existence. This move allows the discussion to proceed without metaphysical commitment, a crucial strategy in cross-school debates.

Guided Path
1
Identify the objection
The occasionalist challenges the notion of active potency: if fire does not really burn (God creates the burning), how can one acknowledge a power in fire?
The objection is based on the Ash‘ari doctrine of occasionalism (al-‘āda).
2
Identify the grounds of the objection
The objector assumes that speaking of 'active potency' implies a real causal power inherent in the object. He rejects this because causal efficacy belongs solely to God.
3
Extract the author's response
The author distinguishes two aims: explaining the meaning of a term (sharh al-ism) versus verifying the existence of the named (tahqiq wujud al-musamma). His purpose is the former, not the latter.
Find the Arabic phrase: غرضنا شرح معنى الاسم لا تحقيق وجود المسمى.
4
Assess the dialectical strategy
The author does not try to refute occasionalism here; he limits his discussion to definitional clarity. He refers those seeking metaphysical resolution to his book Tahafut al-Falasifa.
This strategy prevents the objection from derailing the definitional discussion.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قيل : قولكم : إن الشيء بالقوة لا بالفعل يرجع حاصله إلى الاستعداد للشيء، وقبول المحل له وهذا مفهوم، وأما القوة الأخرى التي هي فاعلة كقوة النار على الإحراق كيف يعترف بها من يرى أن النار لا تحرق، وإنما الله تعالى يخلق الإحراق عند وقوع اللقاء بين القطن والنار مثلاً، بحكم إجراء الله تعالى العادة؟ قلنا : غرضنا لما ذكرنا شرح معنى الاسم لا تحقيق وجود المسمى، وقد نبهنا على وجه تحقيق الحق فيه في كتاب تهافت الفلاسفة والغرض أن لا يلتبس إحداهما بالأخرى إذا استعملهما معتقد ذلك .
If it is said: 'Your statement that a thing is potential not actual reduces to preparation for the thing and the receptacle's acceptance of it, and this is intelligible. But the other potency, which is active – like the potency of fire to burn – how can one who holds that fire does not burn, rather God Most High creates burning when contact occurs between cotton and fire, for example, by the habit of God Most High, acknowledge it?' We say: 'Our purpose in what we mentioned is to explain the meaning of the name, not to verify the existence of the named. And we have alerted to the way of verifying the truth about it in the book Tahafut al-Falasifa. The purpose is that one should not confuse one of them with the other when a believer in that [occasionalist view] uses them.'
Source pages p. 175

Commentary
This passage is a dialectical exchange: the author presents a hypothetical objection (naqd) and then answers it (jawab). The objection comes from an occasionalist perspective, likely that of Ash‘ari theologians: while potentiality as 'preparation for the thing' (i.e., passive potency) is acceptable, the notion of an active potency like fire's power to burn is problematic because, according to occasionalism, fire does not independently cause burning; God creates burning at the moment of contact. The author responds by clarifying that his aim in the previous discussion was purely linguistic and conceptual: to explain the meaning of the term 'active potency' (قوة فاعلة), not to assert the existence of genuine causal powers in natural objects. He refers those seeking a metaphysical resolution to his book Tahafut al-Falasifa (The Incoherence of the Philosophers), where he critiques the philosophers' claims. The immediate goal is to prevent confusion between the two meanings of قوة (potential and active principle) even for someone who denies real natural causation.

Key Terms
قوة فاعلة
استعداد
العادة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the response, what is the author's purpose in describing active potency?
A) To prove that fire truly has causal power
B) To explain the meaning of the term, not its real existence
C) To refute occasionalism
D) To assert that all power belongs to God

Key Takeaway
The author separates definition from ontological commitment, allowing dialectical cooperation with occasionalist opponents.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Construct a similar dialectical exchange in kalam: the objection that 'God creating human actions means humans have no free will'. Write an objection (mu'tarid) and a response (jawab) that distinguishes meaning from existence.
?
Identify in the passage where the author limits his discussion to semantics and find the exact Arabic phrase. Translate it and explain its function.
